5 min
Necessary Attachment of Opposites to a Substrate
Introduces the concept of necessary attachment (luzum) of one opposite to a substrate, with two subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that one opposite may be necessarily attached to a substrate either specifically or non-specifically.

Core Idea
Opposites can be necessarily attached to a substrate, either a specific one or an unspecified one.

The philosophers classify how opposites relate to their substrates. One possibility is that one opposite is necessarily attached. This lesson examines what this means and the two forms it takes: specific attachment (like whiteness to snow) and non-specific attachment (like motion and rest to a body).

Guided Path
1
Read the relevant passage
Read the Arabic text from 'وقد يلزم أحدهما' to 'فأحدهما لا بعينه لازم له'. Then read the translation.
Notice the two cases: 'luzum bi-aynihi' and 'luzum la bi-aynihi'.
2
Identify the two types of attachment
Look for the phrases 'إما بعينه' and 'أو لا بعينه'. Understand that 'specific' means a particular opposite is always present, while 'non-specific' means one of the pair is present but not always the same one.
The example for non-specific is motion and rest for a body.
3
Reflect on the logical implications
Ask: Why does the substrate never become empty of both motion and rest? How does this differ from the case of whiteness and snow?
This shows the necessity of having at least one of the pair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(إما مع اتصافه) أي المحل ( بوسط ) بين المتضادين ) ويعبر عنه ( أي عن ذلك الوسط إما باسم وجودي كالمز المتوسط بين الحلو والحامض، وكالفاتر المتوسط بين الحار والبارد ( أو بسلب الطرفين كما يقال : لا عادل ولا جائر لمن اتصف بحالة متوسطة بين العدل والجور . وأما قولهم الفلك لا ثقيل ولا خفيف فلم يريدوا بسلب الطرفين هناك إثبات متوسطة بين الثقل والخفة ( أو دونه ) أي دون الاتصاف بوسط ( فيخلو ) المحل ( عن الوسط أيضاً ، (كالشفاف) الخالي عن السواد والبياض، وعن كل ما بتوسطهما من الألوان. وأيضاً قد يمكن تعاقبهما ) أي تعاقب الضدين ( على المحل، كالسواد والبياض) بحيث لا يخلو عنهما معاً، بل يعدم أحدهما عنه، ويوجد الآخر فيه في آن واحد كالسواد والبياض ( أو لا يمكن تعاقبهما على المحل بحيث لا يخلو عنهما ، ( كالحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة)، فإنه لا يجوز تعاقبهما على محل ،واحد ( إن قلنا : ( يجب أن يكون ( بينهما سكون ) ، كما هو المشهور.
قوله : ( للجسم) أي المطلق إن جعل حال الحدوث داخلا في السكون، أو الجسم الباقي إن لم يجعل داخلاً فيه، واعتبر فيه اللبث .
قوله : ( كالمز المتوسط ) بناء على أنه طعم بسيط بين الحلاوة والحموضة، وإن حصل من خلط الجسم الحلو والحامض، وكذا الفاتر.
قوله : (إثبات حالة متوسطة ) بل خلوه عنهما .

Fourth Observatory - Eleventh Objective: The two mutually exclusive things are two matters that do not coexist. Fifthly: (They said) i.e., the philosophers: (And one of them may necessarily attach) i.e., one of the two contraries (to the subject, either specifically, like whiteness being necessary for snow, or not specifically, like motion and rest) on the assumption that it is existent (for a body), for it is not free of both of them together, so one of them unspecified is necessary for it. (And the subject may be free of both of them together, so there is no necessity there for either of them at all.) (Either with its being described) i.e., the subject (by a middle state) between the two contraries, and it is expressed about (i.e., about that middle state either by an existential name like the middle taste between sweet and sour, and like the lukewarm intermediate between hot and cold, or by the negation of the two extremes, as is said: 'neither just nor unjust' for one who is described by an intermediate state between justice and injustice. As for their saying: 'the celestial sphere is neither heavy nor light,' they did not intend by the negation of the two extremes there to affirm an intermediate between heaviness and lightness.) (Or without it) i.e., without being described by a middle (so the subject is free of the middle also, like the transparent) which is devoid of blackness and whiteness, and of all the colors that mediate between them. (And also, they may alternate) i.e., the two contraries may alternate (on the subject, like blackness and whiteness) such that it is not free of both of them together, but one of them is annihilated from it and the other exists in it at the same time, like blackness and whiteness. (Or they cannot alternate on the subject such that it is not free of both of them,) like upward and downward motion, for it is not permissible for them to alternate on one subject (if we say: (there must be) between them (rest) as is well-known. His saying: (for the body) i.e., absolute body if the state of coming-to-be is made to enter into rest, or the remaining body if it is not made to enter into it, and persistence is considered in it. His saying: (like the middle taste) based on it being a simple taste between sweetness and sourness, even if it results from mixing a sweet and sour body, and similarly the lukewarm. His saying: (affirmation of an intermediate state) rather it is free of them both. His saying: (and also etc.) another division of the two contraries. His saying: (except) between species of a (single) genus; what is intended by it is the final species, and if he had intended the real species it would have sufficed, but summary is not like detail. His saying: (like whiteness being necessary for snow) the statement that whiteness is necessary for snow is flawed speech; because of the possibility of it becoming yellow, for example, by saffron, but it is a quibble about the example. His saying: (like motion and rest for the body) either absolutely according to one who makes 'becoming' at the beginning of coming-to-be a rest, or for the remaining body according to others. His saying: (and also, they may alternate) this is a division of the two contraries according to another aspect, and the difference between
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies the different ways opposites (mutadāddān) relate to a substrate (maḥall). It begins by noting that one opposite may necessarily attach to the substrate, either specifically (like whiteness to snow) or non-specifically (like motion and rest to a body, where one of them must be present but not necessarily the same one). Alternatively, the substrate may be free of both opposites simultaneously. This absence can occur either with a middle state (wasaṭ) between the two extremes—expressed either by a positive term (such as 'lukewarm' between hot and cold) or by negating both extremes (such as 'neither just nor unjust' for a just middle person)—or without such a middle, as in the case of a transparent object that is neither black nor white nor any intermediate color. The passage then discusses alternation (taʿāqub): opposites may alternate on the substrate, like blackness and whiteness on a surface, where one replaces the other at an instant, or they may not alternate, like upward and downward motion, because if they were to alternate, rest would need to intervene. The marginal notes (qawluh) clarify the examples and address potential objections, such as the fact that snow can be dyed yellow, thus challenging the necessity of whiteness.

Key Terms
المتضادان
المحل
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an opposite is necessary for a substrate, it must always be the same opposite.
Correction: The text shows that necessity can be non-specific: one of the pair must be present, but not a fixed one. For a body, it may be either motion or rest.

Checkpoint
What does 'non-specific attachment' (لزوم لا بعينه) mean according to the text?
A) A specific opposite is always present.
B) One of the two opposites is always present, but it is not predetermined which one.
C) The substrate is free of both opposites.
D) The opposite is only sometimes present.

Key Takeaway
Opposites can be necessarily attached to a substrate in two ways: specifically or non-specifically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart is the substrate (al-mahall) of faith and disbelief. Which opposite is necessarily attached to you right now? Are you in motion towards Allah or at rest in heedlessness? You cannot be without both. Examine your actions today: do they testify to a specific opposite being attached to you? What would change if you made that attachment one of conscious obedience?
?
The text says one opposite is necessarily attached but unspecified for a body regarding motion and rest. In your spiritual life, you are either obeying or disobeying Allah at every moment. Which opposite is necessarily attached to you? Do you know which one is specific? If you are unsure, your deeds will reveal it. How can you ensure that the necessary attachment of your heart is to righteousness, not sin?
?
Snow is necessarily white by its nature. What is your fixed nature? Is it necessarily attached to good or evil? But unlike snow, you can change your nature through repentance. If the opposite attached to you now is disobedience, what step will you take tonight to make obedience the necessary attachment of your heart?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

Simultaneous Absence of Both Opposites
Classifies the absence of both opposites from a substrate into two subcases: with a middle state and without.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scenarios in which a substrate is free from both opposites: with a middle state and without a middle state.

Core Idea
A substrate can be empty of both opposites, either via a middle state or without any middle.

The philosophers next consider the case where the substrate is free from both opposites simultaneously. This can happen in two ways: either the substrate is described by a middle state between the extremes, or it is devoid of any middle state as well. This lesson clarifies these possibilities with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on absence
Read from 'وقد يخلو المحل عنهما معاً' to 'الخالي عن السواد والبياض، وعن كل ما بتوسطهما من الألوان'.
Identify the two subcases: with middle and without middle.
2
Understand how the middle state can be expressed
The middle can be expressed either by a positive term (like 'lukewarm') or by negation of both extremes (like 'neither just nor unjust').
Note the nuance: the celestial sphere is neither heavy nor light, but that is not a middle state; it is absence of both.
3
Compare with the case of no middle state
The transparent is an example where there is no middle; it is simply devoid of black, white, and all intermediate colors.
This shows that absence does not require an intermediate.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage classifies the different ways opposites (mutadāddān) relate to a substrate (maḥall). It begins by noting that one opposite may necessarily attach to the substrate, either specifically (like whiteness to snow) or non-specifically (like motion and rest to a body, where one of them must be present but not necessarily the same one). Alternatively, the substrate may be free of both opposites simultaneously. This absence can occur either with a middle state (wasaṭ) between the two extremes—expressed either by a positive term (such as 'lukewarm' between hot and cold) or by negating both extremes (such as 'neither just nor unjust' for a just middle person)—or without such a middle, as in the case of a transparent object that is neither black nor white nor any intermediate color. The passage then discusses alternation (taʿāqub): opposites may alternate on the substrate, like blackness and whiteness on a surface, where one replaces the other at an instant, or they may not alternate, like upward and downward motion, because if they were to alternate, rest would need to intervene. The marginal notes (qawluh) clarify the examples and address potential objections, such as the fact that snow can be dyed yellow, thus challenging the necessity of whiteness.

Key Terms
الخلو
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that if both opposites are absent, there must be a middle state.
Correction: The text shows that absence can occur without a middle state, as in the transparent example.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of a middle state expressed by negation of extremes?
A) Lukewarm
B) Transparent
C) Neither just nor unjust
D) Heavy

Key Takeaway
A substrate can be free of both opposites with or without a middle state, and the middle can be expressed either positively or by negating extremes.

Logic and Technical Terms

Alternation of Opposites on a Substrate
Discusses the alternation (taʿāqub) of opposites on a substrate, with examples of possible and impossible alternation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of alternation of opposites on a substrate and when it is possible or not.

Core Idea
Opposites can alternate on a substrate, but some like contrary motions cannot alternate without an intervening rest.

The philosophers finally discuss the possibility of opposites alternating on the same substrate. While some opposites can replace each other (like black and white on a surface), others cannot alternate without a middle state, such as upward and downward motion, which require rest in between.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternation passage
Read from 'وأيضاً قد يمكن تعاقبهما' to 'كما هو المشهور'.
Note the examples: blackness/whiteness vs. upward/downward motion.
2
Distinguish possible and impossible alternation
Possible alternation: opposites succeed each other without the substrate being empty of both. Impossible alternation: the two opposites cannot directly succeed each other because a middle state (rest) is needed.
The text says for motion, rest must intervene if we follow the well-known view.
3
Reflect on the logical reason
Why can black and white alternate directly but motion cannot? The text indicates it's due to the nature of motion and the need for a state of rest.
This relates to the concept of 'sukun' (rest) as a necessary intermediate.

Source Text

Fourth Observatory - Eleventh Objective: The two mutually exclusive things are two matters that do not coexist. Fifthly: (They said) i.e., the philosophers: (And one of them may necessarily attach) i.e., one of the two contraries (to the subject, either specifically, like whiteness being necessary for snow, or not specifically, like motion and rest) on the assumption that it is existent (for a body), for it is not free of both of them together, so one of them unspecified is necessary for it. (And the subject may be free of both of them together, so there is no necessity there for either of them at all.) (Either with its being described) i.e., the subject (by a middle state) between the two contraries, and it is expressed about (i.e., about that middle state either by an existential name like the middle taste between sweet and sour, and like the lukewarm intermediate between hot and cold, or by the negation of the two extremes, as is said: 'neither just nor unjust' for one who is described by an intermediate state between justice and injustice. As for their saying: 'the celestial sphere is neither heavy nor light,' they did not intend by the negation of the two extremes there to affirm an intermediate between heaviness and lightness.) (Or without it) i.e., without being described by a middle (so the subject is free of the middle also, like the transparent) which is devoid of blackness and whiteness, and of all the colors that mediate between them. (And also, they may alternate) i.e., the two contraries may alternate (on the subject, like blackness and whiteness) such that it is not free of both of them together, but one of them is annihilated from it and the other exists in it at the same time, like blackness and whiteness. (Or they cannot alternate on the subject such that it is not free of both of them,) like upward and downward motion, for it is not permissible for them to alternate on one subject (if we say: (there must be) between them (rest) as is well-known. His saying: (for the body) i.e., absolute body if the state of coming-to-be is made to enter into rest, or the remaining body if it is not made to enter into it, and persistence is considered in it. His saying: (like the middle taste) based on it being a simple taste between sweetness and sourness, even if it results from mixing a sweet and sour body, and similarly the lukewarm. His saying: (affirmation of an intermediate state) rather it is free of them both. His saying: (and also etc.) another division of the two contraries. His saying: (except) between species of a (single) genus; what is intended by it is the final species, and if he had intended the real species it would have sufficed, but summary is not like detail. His saying: (like whiteness being necessary for snow) the statement that whiteness is necessary for snow is flawed speech; because of the possibility of it becoming yellow, for example, by saffron, but it is a quibble about the example. His saying: (like motion and rest for the body) either absolutely according to one who makes 'becoming' at the beginning of coming-to-be a rest, or for the remaining body according to others. His saying: (and also, they may alternate) this is a division of the two contraries according to another aspect, and the difference between
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies the different ways opposites (mutadāddān) relate to a substrate (maḥall). It begins by noting that one opposite may necessarily attach to the substrate, either specifically (like whiteness to snow) or non-specifically (like motion and rest to a body, where one of them must be present but not necessarily the same one). Alternatively, the substrate may be free of both opposites simultaneously. This absence can occur either with a middle state (wasaṭ) between the two extremes—expressed either by a positive term (such as 'lukewarm' between hot and cold) or by negating both extremes (such as 'neither just nor unjust' for a just middle person)—or without such a middle, as in the case of a transparent object that is neither black nor white nor any intermediate color. The passage then discusses alternation (taʿāqub): opposites may alternate on the substrate, like blackness and whiteness on a surface, where one replaces the other at an instant, or they may not alternate, like upward and downward motion, because if they were to alternate, rest would need to intervene. The marginal notes (qawluh) clarify the examples and address potential objections, such as the fact that snow can be dyed yellow, thus challenging the necessity of whiteness.

Key Terms
التعاقب
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all opposites can directly alternate on the same substrate.
Correction: The text shows that contrary motions cannot alternate without a resting state in between.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why can't upward and downward motion alternate directly on a body?
A) Because they are of different types
B) Because they require a state of rest between them
C) Because they cannot exist in the same instant
D) Because the body cannot change motion

Key Takeaway
Alternation of opposites is possible for some pairs (like colors) but not others (like contrary motions), where a middle state (rest) is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about sudden changes in your character—like turning from anger to patience—do you assume they can happen instantly like black and white, or is there a necessary 'rest' you are neglecting? How might acknowledging that rest change your method of self-improvement?
?
The lesson shows that some contrary motions cannot alternate without a middle state. Reflect on your spiritual life: when you move from heedlessness to mindfulness, have you allowed for the 'rest' of repentance and reflection? What step will you take today to insert that necessary pause?

Logic and Technical Terms

Conditions of True Opposition
Sanusi defines true opposition and clarifies its conditions, addressing the case of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conditions for true opposition (al-taḍādd): that the two species belong to the same proximate genus and that they are final species, and recognize apparent counterexamples like good and evil as accidental or privative opposition.

Core Idea
True opposition requires a shared genus and final species; good and evil are not true opposites.

Before diving into arguments about the attributes of Allah, Islamic theologians needed a precise metaphysical vocabulary. One key concept is 'opposition' (taḍādd). This passage from Sanusi's Sharḥ al-Mawāqif sets down the exact conditions under which two things can be truly opposite, and it clears up a common confusion about pairs like good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the main definition
Read the first few sentences of the Arabic passage that define true opposition. Notice the emphasis on 'species of one genus' and 'final species'.
The author uses the example of blackness and whiteness under color.
2
Understand the conditions
Identify the two conditions: (1) the two things must be species of a single genus, and (2) they must be final species (not generic categories themselves).
Final species are those that cannot be further divided into sub-species.
3
Examine the counterexample
The passage addresses the apparent counterexample of good and evil. Read the discussion carefully to see why they are not true opposites.
The text explains that they fall under privation and possession or accidental opposition.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. It prevents us from misapplying the concept of opposition to pairs that are not truly symmetric.
This precision is important for later theological arguments about divine attributes.

Fourth Observatory - Eleventh Objective: The two mutually exclusive things are two matters that do not coexist. Species that are not subsumed under one genus—rather, opposition is between species subsumed under it. (And opposition is not) in these species (except between the final species subsumed under one proximate genus, like blackness and whiteness subsumed under color, which is their proximate genus) (And what is imagined contrary to that, like virtue and vice and like good and evil, is due to privation and possession or opposition accidentally). Some thought that good and evil are two contraries despite being two genera for many species under them, so the statement that there is no opposition between genera would not be valid, and it is false; because evil has no existential nature. And assuming it were so, then nothing of evilness or goodness is essential to what is under them; because goodness is the thing being suitable, and evilness is the thing being unsuitable. And we can conceive of things designated as good and evil while being oblivious to their being good or evil, so they are not genera for what is under them. And others thought that courage, while being under a genus... His saying: (between genera) i.e., insofar as they are genera, so it is not objected that genera may be species of one genus, like the four divisions of quality, so how can avoidance be valid by his saying 'except between species of one genus'? His saying: (at all) whether they are subsumed under a genus or not, like the highest genera. His saying: (under one genus) rather, under two genera. His saying: (that good and evil) whether they are interpreted as perfection and deficiency, or as suitable and unsuitable. His saying: (two contraries) it is not hidden that their being two contraries requires that they be a qualification from one aspect, in the definition of the two mutually exclusive things to include them also, because they can both occur in one thing from two aspects. His saying: (existential) i.e., negation is not taken in its concept, because it is an expression for the absence of good. His saying: 'so nothing is... etc.' i.e., we do not accept that they are essential to what is under them, so the refutation by them against our statement 'no opposition between genera' is not valid. But if a refutation is brought by them against our statement 'no opposition except between final species', the answer is the first. His saying: (because goodness etc.) is a support for the denial, presented in the form of reasoning, for promotion and indication of the strength of the denial. So the objection to his statement 'and we can conceive of things etc.' is that conception by the innermost essence is precluded, and conception by the aspect does not benefit negating essentiality, outside the law of debate. The divisions of the two divisions according to aspects, so the coexistence of possibility of alternation with the necessity of one of them unspecified for the subject in one matter, for example, does not harm. His saying: (while being oblivious to their being good or evil) this is only completed if the conception of those things by the innermost essence is established, and it is in the realm of prevention. So the closest in reasoning is to say: what is established for a thing as measured to another is not essential to it, and goodness and likewise evilness are of this kind.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines true opposition (al-taḍādd) and its conditions. It states that opposition only occurs between species that are under one genus, and specifically between the final species (al-anwāʿ al-akhīra) under the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarīb). For example, blackness and whiteness are species under the genus color. The text then addresses potential counterexamples, such as virtue and vice, or good and evil. Some might think these are contrary genera, but the passage explains that they are not true opposites: good and evil are described as privation and possession (al-ʿadam wa al-milka) or accidental opposition. Goodness is suitability (mulāʾama) and evil is unsuitability (munāfara); they are not essential properties of their instances. The marginal notes (qawluh) further clarify: the statement 'between genera' is qualified to avoid misunderstanding; genera themselves can be species of higher genera, but opposition is only between species. The notes also discuss the objection about good and evil being genera, and respond that they are not essential—they are relational attributes. The passage ends with a discussion of the conceivability of things as good or evil while ignoring that attribute, showing that goodness/evilness is not part of the essence.

الأنواع الأخيرة
الجنس القريب
العدم والملكة
بالعرض

Correction: They are not true opposites because they arise from privation and possession or accidental opposition; evil has no existential nature, and goodness/evilness are relational, not essential.

Checkpoint

C) They are from privation and possession or accidental opposition.
D) They are final species under a single genus.

Key Takeaway
True opposition only occurs between final species of a single proximate genus; good and evil are not true opposites because they are private or accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does knowing that evil has no existential nature change the way you judge yourself when you fall into sin? Will you now see your wrong actions as absences of good rather than fixed stains?
?
The passage says goodness is 'suitability' (ملاءمة) and evilness is 'unsuitability' (منافرة). Think of a time you called something 'evil'—was it truly unsuitable for your nature or just uncomfortable? Can you name one thing you currently call 'evil' in your life that might actually be a missed suitability? What will you do about it this week?
?
If evil is not an essential quality but a relational absence, then your own capacity for evil is not a fixed part of you. Are there habits or thoughts you've labeled as 'just the way I am' that are really just privations of good? What step can you take today to replace one such absence with its opposite virtue?

Definition of the Two Contraries and the Condition of Tadadd
Sanusi defines al-mutaqabilan and states that tadadd is only between last species under one proximate genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the two contraries (al-mutaqabilan) and the precise condition under which genuine opposition (tadadd) occurs between types.

Core Idea
Opposition requires a single proximate genus and only happens between the lowest species.

In this passage, Sanusi lays down the logical foundation for understanding opposition. He defines what it means for two things to be contraries and then narrows down where genuine tadadd can occur. This clears up a common confusion about whether opposition applies to genera or species.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and condition
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition of the two contraries and the condition that tadadd only occurs between the last species under one proximate genus.
The term 'al-mutaqabilan' refers to any two things that cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.
2
Study the example
Observe the example of blackness and whiteness, which are last species under the proximate genus 'color'. This illustrates the condition.
The example shows that tadadd does not occur between genera or between species that share only a distant genus.
3
Understand the exclusion of genera
The commentary clarifies that tadadd is not between genera as such, even if genera can be considered types of a higher genus.
This prevents the objection that genera like the four qualities are types under one genus but not in tadadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان أنواع ليست مندرجة تحت جنس واحد، إنما التضاد بين الأنواع المندرجة تحته . ( ولا يكون ) التضاد في هذه الأنواع ) إلا بين الأنواع الأخيرة المندرجة تحت جنس واحد قريب كالسواد والبياض المندرجين تحت اللون الذي هو جنسهما القريب قوله : ( بين الأجناس) أي من حيث إنها أجناس، فلا يرد أن الأجناس قد تكون أنواع جنس واحد، كالأقسام الأربعة للكيف، فكيف يصح الاحتراز عنها بقوله إلا بين أنواع جنس واحد ؟ . قوله : (أصلاً) سواء كانت مندرجة تحت جنس أو لا، كالأجناس العالية. قوله : (تحت جنس واحد بل تحت جنسين .
The fourth observation - the eleventh aim: The two contraries (al-mutaqabilan) are two things that do not come together. Types (anwa') not subsumed under one genus; rather opposition (al-tadadd) is among the types subsumed under it. And opposition in these types is only between the last types (al-anwa' al-akhira) subsumed under one proximate genus, like blackness and whiteness subsumed under color, which is their proximate genus. His statement: (between the genera) meaning insofar as they are genera, so it is not objected that genera may be types of one genus, like the four divisions of quality; so how is the exclusion by his statement 'except between types of one genus' valid? His statement: (absolutely) whether they are subsumed under a genus or not, like the highest genera. His statement: (under one genus but under two genera).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the two contraries and clarifies the condition for genuine opposition (tadadd). The author first states that the two contraries are two things that cannot coexist together. He then explains that opposition does not occur between types that are not under a single genus; rather, it occurs only among types subsumed under a common genus. The crucial point is that tadadd is only between the last (lowest) species under one proximate genus, using the example of blackness and whiteness under the genus color. The commentator then explains the text: when the author says 'between the genera', he means insofar as they are genera, so there is no opposition between genera as such (even if they are types of a higher genus). He also explains 'absolutely' (aslan) referring to whether they are under a genus or not, like the highest genera. Finally, he clarifies a possible misreading: the text says 'under one genus but under two genera', emphasizing that the condition requires a single proximate genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a contradiction you perceive in your own spiritual life—perhaps between hope in Allah’s mercy and fear of His punishment. Using the condition of tadadd, ask yourself: Are these truly opposite under the same proximate genus? What is that common genus, and are they the last species under it? Could it be that they are not actually contradictory but different levels of the same reality? How might this logical insight change how you hold both? This week, when you feel torn between two religious emotions, pause and identify their common source. Write down one concrete step to integrate them more harmoniously.
?
Consider a time you dismissed a fellow Muslim’s practice as wrong because it seemed opposed to yours. Did you first check whether both practices are last species under the same proximate genus of Islamic obligation? If not, you may have misapplied tadadd where it does not apply. Before your next judgment, ask: Are these actions truly under one proximate genus? If they are not, they cannot be true contraries. Commit to suspending judgment until you find the common genus—or accept that they may coexist.

Good and Evil Are Not True Contraries
The commentator refutes that good and evil are contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why good and evil are not true contraries: they can coexist under different aspects, and evil is a privation, not a positive entity.

Core Idea
Good and evil are relational, not contrary essences.

The objector claims that good and evil are true contraries, which would challenge the principle that no opposition exists between genera. The commentator clarifies the definitions and shows that evil is merely the absence of good, so it cannot be a positive contrary. Additionally, good and evil can coexist in the same thing from different aspects.

Guided Path
1
Understand definitions
Good and evil can be interpreted as perfection/deficiency or agreeable/disagreeable. Recognize that these interpretations are relative.
2
Condition for contraries
For two things to be contraries, they must be opposed under the same aspect. Good and evil can be present in one thing from two aspects, so they are not true contraries.
3
Evil as privation
Evil has no existential reality; it is the absence of good. Therefore, it cannot be a positive contrary to good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أن الخير والشر ) سواء فسرا بالكمال والنقصان، أو بالملائم والمنافر. قوله : (ضدان) لا يخفى أن كونهما ضدين يقتضي أن يكون قيد من جهة واحدة، في تعريف المتقابلين لإدخالهما أيضاً لاجتماعهما في شيء واحد من جهتين. قوله : ( وجودية) أي لا يكون مأخوذاً في مفهومه السلب؛ لأنه عبارة عن عدم الخير. :قوله فليس شيء إلخ ) أي لا نسلم كونهما ذاتيين لما تحتهما، فلا يرد النقض بهما على قولنا : لا تضاد بين الأجناس وأما إذا أورد النقض بهما على قولنا : لا تضاد إلا بين الأنواع الأخيرة فالجواب هو الأول. : قوله : ( لأن الخيرية إلخ) سند للمنع أورده بصورة الاستدلال؛ ترويجاً وإشارة إلى قوة المنع، فالإيراد على قوله : وقد تعقل الأشياء إلخ، بأن التعقل بالكنه ممنوع، والتعقل بالوجه لا يفيد نفي الذاتية خارج عن قانون المناظرة . أقسام التقسيمين بالحيثيات، فلا يضر اجتماع إمكان التعاقب مع لزوم أحدهما لا يعينه للمحل في مادة واحدة مثلاً. قوله : (مع الذهول عن كونها خيرات أو شرور هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت تعقل تلك الأشياء بالكنه، وهو في حيز المنع، فالأقرب في الاستدلال أن يقال: ما ثبت للشيء مقيساً إلى الغير لا يكون ذاتياً ،له والخيرية وكذا الشرية من هذا القبيل .
His statement: (that good (al-khayr) and evil (al-sharr)) whether they are interpreted as perfection and deficiency, or as agreeable and disagreeable. His statement: (two contraries) it is not hidden that their being two contraries requires that the condition be from one aspect, in the definition of the two contraries to also include them due to their coming together in one thing from two aspects. His statement: (existential) meaning negation is not taken in its concept because it is the absence of good. His statement: 'so nothing is intrinsic etc.' meaning we do not grant that they are intrinsic (dhatiyyan) to what is under them, so the objection is not raised against our statement: no opposition between genera. And if the objection is raised against our statement: no opposition except between the last types, then the answer is the first. His statement: (because goodness (al-khairiyya) etc.) is a support for the prevention presented in the form of inference; for promotion and indication of the strength of the prevention, so the objection against his statement: 'And things are conceived etc.' is that conception of the essence (al-ta'aqqul bi al-kunh) is prohibited, and conception by aspect (al-ta'aqqul bi al-wajh) does not benefit negation of intrinsicality outside the law of debate. The divisions of the two divisions by aspects, so it does not harm the combination of possibility of succession with the necessity of one of them not being specific to the locus in one matter for example. His statement: (with disregard of their being goods or evils) this is only valid if the conception of those things by essence is established, and it is in the realm of prevention, so the closest inference is to say: what is established for a thing in relation to another is not intrinsic to it, and goodness and likewise evil are of this sort.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the objection that good (al-khayr) and evil (al-sharr) are true contraries (diddan), which would violate the claim that there is no opposition between genera. The commentator first clarifies that the terms 'good' and 'evil' can be interpreted either as perfection and deficiency or as agreeable and disagreeable. He then explains that for two things to be true contraries, they must be opposed under the same aspect, but good and evil can coexist in one thing from different aspects (e.g., something can be good in one respect and evil in another). Next, he addresses the nature of evil: it has no existential reality (it is the absence of good), so it cannot be a positive contrary. He then argues that goodness and evil are not intrinsic (dhati) properties of the things they are attributed to, because they are relational (based on agreement or disagreement). Therefore, the objection does not refute the principle of no opposition between genera. The commentator also discusses epistemological issues: if one claims to conceive things like pleasure or pain without considering their goodness or evil, this conception is only by aspect, not by essence, and thus does not prove that goodness/evil are not essential. The final point is that any property that belongs to a thing relative to another is not intrinsic to it.

Key Terms
الخير (al-khayr)
الشر (al-sharr)
العدم والملكة (al-'adam wa al-milka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think good and evil are intrinsic, opposite essences that cannot coexist.
Correction: The passage shows they are relational and can coexist under different aspects, and evil is merely the absence of good.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that good and evil are not true contraries?
A) Because they cannot coexist at all
B) Because they are both genera and species simultaneously
C) Because evil is a privation and goodness is relational, not intrinsic
D) Because they are always opposites under the same aspect

Key Takeaway
Good and evil are not true contraries because they can coexist under different aspects and evil is a privation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have labeled something as purely evil, failing to see any good in it from another perspective? How can you train yourself to look for that other aspect?
?
Since evil is the absence of good, what good are you neglecting that leaves a void for evil to fill? Identify one specific good you will actively pursue this week.
?
Think of a person you have considered entirely bad. Can you find one good quality they possess? How does acknowledging that coexistence of good and evil change your attitude toward them?

Goodness and Evil Are Not Intrinsic but Relational
The commentator argues that goodness and evil are not intrinsic but relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why goodness and evil are not intrinsic (dhati) properties but are relational, and the epistemological distinction between conceiving by essence and by aspect.

Core Idea
Goodness and evil are relational, not essential attributes.

After establishing that good and evil are not true contraries, the commentator addresses the deeper objection that they might be intrinsic (dhati) to the things they qualify. He argues that any property that belongs to something only in relation to another cannot be intrinsic. Additionally, he deals with the epistemological objection that if we can conceive things without considering their goodness or evil, then these are not essential. The commentator responds that such conception is only by aspect, not by essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The objector may argue that goodness and evil are intrinsic to things like pleasure and pain. The commentator denies this.
2
Relational nature
Goodness and evil are based on agreement or disagreement with a nature. They are relational, not essential.
3
Epistemological objection
If one claims to conceive things by essence without considering their goodness or evil, the commentator responds that conception by essence is not possible here; only conception by aspect is possible.
4
Conclusion
The fundamental principle: what is established for a thing relative to another is not intrinsic to it.

Virtue and Vice: Accidental, Not Essential Opposites
Refutation of the claim that virtue and vice are true contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that virtue and vice are true contraries is refuted: the opposition lies in accidental attributes, not in the realities of actions.

Core Idea
Opposition between virtue and vice is accidental, not essential.

The commentator addresses an objection: if courage (a virtue) and recklessness (a vice) are opposites, they might seem to violate the principle that only items under the same genus can be contraries. He shows that the opposition is between their accidental qualities, not their real natures. Courage, as a middle state, is not an extreme opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the core passage
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection and the refutation. Notice the key terms: فضيلة, رذيلة, شجاعة, تهور, and عارض.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that virtue and vice are true opposites, giving the example of courage and recklessness. Why would this be problematic for the principle of opposition?
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that each of courage and recklessness has a reality (حقيقة), and the attributes of being virtue or vice are accidental (عارض) to them. Opposition is between these accidents, not between the essences.
Also note that courage is a middle state, so it is not extremely distant from either extreme.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this refutation preserve the rule that true opposition only occurs between species of the same genus?
Accidental attributes can be considered as a new genus for the purpose of opposition.

The fourth observation - the eleventh aim: The two contraries are two things that do not come together. Virtue (al-fadila) is opposed to recklessness (al-tahawwur) which is subsumed under the genus of vice (al-radhila), so it is not correct to say that there is no opposition between types subsumed under different genera. It is also refuted because each of courage (al-shaja'a) and recklessness has a reality, and an attribute has accidentally occurred to it, namely being virtue or vice, and there is no opposition between their realities, since one is not at the extreme distance from the other. If you wish to apply what is in the book to it, you say: His statement 'like virtue and vice' refers to the second misconception to which he indicated the answer with his statement 'or opposition in it accidentally'. His statement: (indication of the second misconception and the departure from what is in al-Mulakhkhas to indicate that the objection is not specific to recklessness and courage, but the other extremes are also likewise, and their mention in al-Mulakhkhas is merely for exemplification. His statement: (accidentally (bi al-'arad)) meaning by way of following, not essentially; because essential opposition is between their two accidents, and there is no need to make the ba' meaning 'in' and divert the expression from the apparent. His statement: (indication of the first misconception) so what is meant by good and evil is their concepts; since there is not opposition between everything they apply to. His statement: (opposed to recklessness etc.) recklessness is a quality by which one dares to do what does not benefit except bringing harm to its possessor, so it is a kind of madness, and madness is of various kinds. His statement: (and an attribute has accidentally occurred to it etc.) The commentator said in the margins of al-Mutali': Even if we grant that they are two types for them, we do not grant that they are opposites, because the discussion is about real opposition (al-tadadd al-haqiqi), and courage is a middle between recklessness and cowardice, so it is not an opposite to either of them. His statement: (since one is not at the extreme distance etc.) This does not indicate the negation of opposition absolutely, but the negation of real opposition; and you have known that the discussion is about that, so no problem. His statement: (indication of the second misconception) so in the expression there is an ellipsis of the genitive, i.e., 'like the two types of virtue and vice', and accepting this ellipsis is more beneficial for the multiplicity of the question then, contrary to the second explanation.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that virtue and vice are true contraries, using courage (under virtue) and recklessness (under vice) as an example. The commentator first presents the objection: one might think that because courage (shaja'a) and recklessness (tahawwur) are opposites and belong to different genera (virtue and vice), this violates the principle that there is no opposition between types under different genera. The answer is that each of courage and recklessness has a real essence, and the properties of being virtue or vice are accidental ('arad) to them; the real opposition is between these accidental qualities, not between the essences. Courage and recklessness are not at extreme distances from each other (courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice). The commentator then explains how this relates to the text: the phrase 'like virtue and vice' indicates the second misconception, answered by 'or opposition in it accidentally'. The commentary further clarifies that the accidental attributes are relational (i'tibari) and have no independent reality; they are mere concepts. Thus, they can be considered as the last species under a common genus (the more general concept of an attribute), so they do not violate the rule. The commentator also quotes from the margins of al-Mutali' to reinforce that courage is a middle point and thus not a true opposite. Finally, he notes that in the expression 'like virtue and vice', there is an ellipsis meaning 'like the two types of virtue and vice', which allows multiple interpretations.

الرذيلة (al-radhila)
الشجاعة (al-shaja'a)
التهور (al-tahawwur)
العارض (al-'arad)
التضاد الحقيقي (al-tadadd al-haqiqi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that virtue and vice are essential opposites, meaning that courage and recklessness are directly opposite by nature.
Correction: The passage clarifies that courage and recklessness are not opposite in their essences; the opposition is between the accidental attributes of virtue and vice. Courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice, so it is not a true opposite.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say courage and recklessness are not true contraries?
A) Because they are under different genera
B) Because opposition is between their accidental properties, not their realities
C) Because both are virtues
D) Because they are at extreme distances from each other

Key Takeaway
Virtue and vice are accidental attributes to actions; the real opposition is between these accidents, not between the actions' essences, and courage is a middle state, not an opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've learned that courage and recklessness are not opposites by essence but by accident. Think of an action you recently performed that you labeled 'courageous.' Could it have been closer to recklessness or cowardice? Where would you place it on the spectrum? Commit today to honestly re-evaluate that action, and ask someone you trust to help you see your blind spots.
?
If virtue and vice are accidental, not essential, then no action is inherently 'evil' or 'good' in its essence. Does this comfort you when you fail, or does it challenge you to stop excusing your faults as 'just the way you are'? Choose one recurring fault and deliberately train yourself to view it as an accidental habit that can be changed—not a fixed part of your identity.
?
The commentary says courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice. What is the middle in your own life when it comes to facing difficulties? Are you more prone to rush in without thought (recklessness) or to shrink back (cowardice)? Spend five minutes today mapping out one specific challenge you are currently facing, identifying the two extremes and the courageous middle action you can actually take.

Relational Accidents and the Middle State of Courage
Explaining the relational nature of accidental attributes and the middle state of courage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the accidental attributes of virtue and vice are relational (i'tibari) concepts, and that courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice, reinforcing that it is not a true opposite.

Core Idea
Accidental attributes like virtue and vice are mental concepts, not real entities.

Having established that opposition is accidental, the commentator further clarifies that these accidents are relational (i'tibari) — they have no independent reality. He also explains, via a quotation from al-Mutali', that courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice, so it cannot be an opposite to either. This section shows how these clarifications apply to the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed commentary
Read the Arabic passage that explains the relational nature of accidents and the middle state. Pay attention to the term 'اعتباري' and the discussion of courage.
2
Understand 'i'tibari' accidents
The commentator says these accidents are 'اعتباريان' (relational), meaning they are mere concepts without independent reality. They can serve as a common genus for opposition.
This avoids the objection that virtue and vice are not under one genus.
3
Grasp the middle state of courage
The commentator quotes from the margins of al-Mutali' that courage is a middle between recklessness and cowardice. Thus, it cannot be a true opposite to either, as true opposites require extreme distance.
4
Apply to the text
The commentator shows how phrases like 'virtue and vice' and 'good and evil' in the original text are interpreted: they refer to the types or concepts, not the actual instances.
This requires careful adaptation (عناية وتصرف).

The fourth observation - the eleventh aim: The two contraries are two things that do not come together. Virtue (al-fadila) is opposed to recklessness (al-tahawwur) which is subsumed under the genus of vice (al-radhila), so it is not correct to say that there is no opposition between types subsumed under different genera. It is also refuted because each of courage (al-shaja'a) and recklessness has a reality, and an attribute has accidentally occurred to it, namely being virtue or vice, and there is no opposition between their realities, since one is not at the extreme distance from the other. Rather, opposition is between their accidents. This is what was mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas. If you wish to apply what is in the book to it, you say: His statement 'like virtue and vice' refers to the second misconception to which he indicated the answer with his statement 'or opposition in it accidentally'. And his statement 'and like good and evil' refers to the first misconception to which he indicated the first answer from the two answers of al-Mulakhkhas with his statement 'so it is from privation and possession (al-'adam wa al-milka)'. And you may say: He intended. His statement: (at the extreme distance) for it is between the two extremes, namely recklessness and cowardice (al-jubn). His statement: (only) opposition is between their accidents etc. And these two accidents are relational (i'tibariyan); they have no reality except the two mentioned concepts, so the more general matter considered as genus for them, and they are two last types with respect to their instances, so the objection is not raised against our statement: no opposition except between the last types of one genus. His statement: (if you wish etc.) in it is indication that the application requires a kind of care and adaptation, such that by his statement 'like virtue and vice' is meant what they apply to, and by his statement 'and good and evil' their concepts. His statement: (indication of the second misconception and the departure from what is in al-Mulakhkhas to indicate that the objection is not specific to recklessness and courage, but the other extremes are also likewise, and their mention in al-Mulakhkhas is merely for exemplification. His statement: (accidentally (bi al-'arad)) meaning by way of following, not essentially; because essential opposition is between their two accidents, and there is no need to make the ba' meaning 'in' and divert the expression from the apparent. His statement: (indication of the first misconception) so what is meant by good and evil is their concepts; since there is not opposition between everything they apply to. His statement: (opposed to recklessness etc.) recklessness is a quality by which one dares to do what does not benefit except bringing harm to its possessor, so it is a kind of madness, and madness is of various kinds. His statement: (and an attribute has accidentally occurred to it etc.) The commentator said in the margins of al-Mutali': Even if we grant that they are two types for them, we do not grant that they are opposites, because the discussion is about real opposition (al-tadadd al-haqiqi), and courage is a middle between recklessness and cowardice, so it is not an opposite to either of them. His statement: (since one is not at the extreme distance etc.) This does not indicate the negation of opposition absolutely, but the negation of real opposition; and you have known that the discussion is about that, so no problem. His statement: (indication of the second misconception) so in the expression there is an ellipsis of the genitive, i.e., 'like the two types of virtue and vice', and accepting this ellipsis is more beneficial for the multiplicity of the question then, contrary to the second explanation.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that virtue and vice are true contraries, using courage (under virtue) and recklessness (under vice) as an example. The commentator first presents the objection: one might think that because courage (shaja'a) and recklessness (tahawwur) are opposites and belong to different genera (virtue and vice), this violates the principle that there is no opposition between types under different genera. The answer is that each of courage and recklessness has a real essence, and the properties of being virtue or vice are accidental ('arad) to them; the real opposition is between these accidental qualities, not between the essences. Courage and recklessness are not at extreme distances from each other (courage is a middle state between recklessness and cowardice). The commentator then explains how this relates to the text: the phrase 'like virtue and vice' indicates the second misconception, answered by 'or opposition in it accidentally'. The commentary further clarifies that the accidental attributes are relational (i'tibari) and have no independent reality; they are mere concepts. Thus, they can be considered as the last species under a common genus (the more general concept of an attribute), so they do not violate the rule. The commentator also quotes from the margins of al-Mutali' to reinforce that courage is a middle point and thus not a true opposite. Finally, he notes that in the expression 'like virtue and vice', there is an ellipsis meaning 'like the two types of virtue and vice', which allows multiple interpretations.

الرذيلة (al-radhila)
الشجاعة (al-shaja'a)
التهور (al-tahawwur)
الجبن (al-jubn)
العارض (al-'arad)
الاعتباري (al-i'tibari)

First Two Objections to Induction
Two objections and answers to induction proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two objections to the induction proof of opposition (circularity and necessity of utmost difference) and the responses to them.

Core Idea
Induction must be defended against logical circularity and the nature of utmost difference.

The induction proof for opposition faces several challenges. This lesson covers the first two: the charge of circular reasoning and the question of whether utmost difference is necessary. The text presents each objection clearly followed by a concise response, revealing a careful dialectical process within Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the entire Arabic passage for the first two objections and their answers. Then use the translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice the structure: objection then 'والجواب'.
2
Analyze the circularity objection
Understand why the first objection claims circularity: induction about opposition assumes the very classification it seeks to prove.
The answer appeals to 'absence of utmost difference' as an independent criterion.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection argues that if utmost difference is stipulated, then opposition between two species is necessary, not inductive. If not stipulated, the claim is false.
The response denies the necessity, allowing two equal things to be at utmost difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثبت بالاستقراء) فإن البرهان الذي أورده على هذا المطلب لا يتم، لكن اعترض على إثباته بالاستقراء أيضاً بوجوه الأول أن معنى الاستقراء في انحصار التضاد بين نوعين من جنس هو أنا وجدناه فيما بينهما دون غيرهما، ولا طريق إلى نفيه عن الفجور والعفة مثلا، سوى أنه لا يكون إلا فيما بين نوعين من جنس واحد وهذان نوعان من جنسين، وفيه دور ظاهر. والجواب : أن الطريق إلى ذلك انتفاء غاية الخلاف بينهما . الثاني: أنه إن اشترط في التضاد غاية الخلاف، فكونه فيما بين نوعين دون أنواع من جنس ضروري لا استقرائي؛ لأن غاية الخلاف إنما يكون بين الطرفين لا بين الطرف وبعض الأوساط . وإن لم يشترط فبطلانه ظاهر، كما في أنواع اللون والجواب : منع الضرورة؛ إذ العقل يجوز أن يكون شيئان متساويان ويكونان معاً في غاية . الخلاف الثالث : الاستقراء هو الذي دل على انتفائه الرابع : أنهم أطبقوا على تضاد السواد . والبياض على الإطلاق مع أنهما ليسا نوعين آخرين من اللون بل السوادات المتفاوتة أنواع مختلفة مشتركة في عارض السواد المقول بالتشكيك، وكذا البياض. فعلى ما ذكروا من أن التضاد الحقيقي لا يكون إلا بين نوعين بينهما غاية الخلاف يلزم أن لا يكون في الألوان إلا بين غاية السواد وغاية البياض، ويمكن منع اختلاف السوادات والبياضات بالنوع، وإن كان مطلق السواد والبياض عارضاً لما تحته .
His saying: '(established by induction)' – for the proof he presented for this claim is not perfect, but objections were raised against its establishment by induction as well, in several ways. First: The meaning of induction in confining opposition between two species of a genus is that we find it between them and not between others. There is no way to deny it regarding licentiousness and chastity, for example, except that it occurs only between two species of one genus, and these two are species of two genera, and therein is an apparent circularity. The answer: The way to that is the absence of utmost difference between them. Second: If utmost difference is stipulated in opposition, then its being between two species and not between species of a genus is necessary, not inductive; because utmost difference is only between the extremes, not between an extreme and some means. But if it is not stipulated, then its falsity is apparent, as in the types of color. The answer: Denial of necessity; for the intellect allows that two things be equal and together in utmost difference. Third: Induction is what indicates its denial. Fourth: They unanimously agreed on the opposition of black and white absolutely, although they are not two final species of color, but rather the varying blacks are different species sharing in the accident of blackness said by analogy, and likewise whiteness. So according to what they mentioned that real opposition occurs only between two species between which there is utmost difference, it would follow that in colors there is opposition only between the extreme black and extreme white. And it is possible to deny the difference of blacks and whites in species, even if absolute black and white are accidents of what is under them.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. It critiques the induction proof that a real opposite is unique. Four objections are raised. First objection: Induction about opposition being between two species of a genus leads to circularity because to deny opposition between e.g. licentiousness and chastity, one must assume they are from two genera. Answer: The way to know is the absence of utmost difference. Second objection: If utmost difference is required, then opposition between two species is necessary, not inductive. If not required, it is false. Answer: The intellect can conceive two equal things both at utmost difference. Third objection (fragmentary): Induction itself shows its negation. Fourth objection: There is consensus that black and white are opposites absolutely, but they are not final species; varying blacks are different species sharing the accident 'black' by analogy. This would imply only extreme black and white are opposites, which is problematic. Response: One can deny that blacks differ in species, even if absolute black is an accident.

Key Terms
استقراء
تضاد
غاية الخلاف
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Induction for opposition requires a non-circular criterion like utmost difference, and the necessary status of that criterion is debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on inductive reasoning in your daily life (e.g., observing patterns in people or events), have you ever paused to check if your reasoning is circular—assuming what you are trying to prove? What would it look like to apply the test of 'utmost difference' to ensure genuine opposition?
?
The second objection challenges whether the intellect can conceive of two equal things being at utmost difference. Think of a belief you hold about God's attributes—do you assume any of them are mutually exclusive? How can this lesson's denial of necessity open you to a deeper, less presumptuous understanding of divine unity?

Third and Fourth Objections to Induction
Third and fourth objections to induction proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth objections to the induction proof—that induction itself negates it and that the example of black and white creates a problem—and the brief response.

Core Idea
Induction is self-negating unless careful distinctions about species are made.

The final two objections challenge the induction proof more directly. The third objection claims that induction itself shows its own negation. The fourth raises a problem from the consensus that black and white are opposites, yet they are not final species. The response addresses the classification of black and white.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
The third objection is fragmentary: 'الاستقراء هو الذي دل على انتفائه' meaning induction itself indicates its negation. No explicit answer is given in the text.
This may be incomplete; in context, it challenges the reliability of induction.
2
Study the fourth objection
The fourth objection points out that black and white are universally considered opposites, yet they are not final species but share an accident (blackness/whiteness). This contradicts the requirement that opposites be two species of a genus.
The answer allows that blacks and whites may not differ in species, preserving the rule.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on how this objection forces a refinement: only extreme black and extreme white are opposites, and the variation within black/white is accidental, not specific.

Source Text

Understanding Privation and Possession
Defines 'adam wa malaka and distinguishes its two types: mashhooriyan and haqiqiyan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'adam wa malaka and the distinction between its two types: common (mashhooriyan) and real (haqiqiyan).

Core Idea
Privation and possession requires a subject capable of the quality.

This lesson introduces a key logical concept: things that are opposites in a special way. Unlike simple contradictions, privation and possession have conditions about the subject's capacity. You'll learn how scholars distinguish between common cases like a beardless man and real cases like blindness in a scorpion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms: 'adam wa malaka, mashhooriyan, haqiqiyan.
Pay attention to the examples—they clarify the abstract definitions.
2
Identify the two types
The passage divides privation and possession into two types: common and real. For each, see how the 'acceptance of the existent thing' is considered differently.
Common privation looks at a specific time; real privation considers the nature or genus of the subject.
3
Understand the real type's condition
The passage ends with a key point: for real privation, the impossibility of coexistence is not enough; the relation to the subject must be part of the concept of the privative term.
This separates real from common privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان. والعدم والملكة: فإن اعتبر قبوله للأمر الوجودي في ذلك الوقت كالكوسج، وهو عدم اللحية عمن شأنه في ذلك الوقت أن يكون ملتحياً لا للأمرد الذي ليس من شأنه اللحية في ذلك الوقت، فهو العدم والملكة المشهوريان. وإن اعتبر قبوله له أعم من ذلك، بل بحسب نوعه كالعمى للأكمه، وعدم اللحية للمرأة، أو جنسه القريب أو البعيد، فالأول كالعمى للعقرب فإن البصر من شأن جنسها القريب أعني الحيوان، والثاني كالسكون المقابل للحركة الإرادية للجبل، فإن جنسه البعيد أعني الجسم الذي هو فوق الجماد قابل للحركة الإرادية، لا كعدم القيام بالغير للمفارق إذ ليس من شأن المفارق القيام بالغير، ولا من شأن نوعه أو جنسه مطلقاً إذ لم يجعل الجوهر جنساً له، فهو العدم والملكة الحقيقيان. فالحقيقي من العدم والملكة لا يكفي فيه امتناع الاجتماع بالنسبة إلى الموضوع القابل، بل لا بد مع ذلك أن تكون النسبة إليه مأخوذة في مفهوم العدمي.
The Fourth Observatory - The Eleventh Objective: The two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one essence. And privation and possession: if its acceptance of the existent thing is considered in that time, like the beardless man, which is the absence of a beard from one whose nature is to be bearded at that time, not from an adolescent who is not naturally bearded at that time, then it is the common privation and possession. And if its acceptance of it is considered more general than that, but according to its type, like blindness for the one born blind, and absence of beard for a woman, or according to its proximate or remote genus, the first like blindness for the scorpion, for sight is of the nature of its proximate genus, namely animal, and the second like rest opposite voluntary motion for a mountain, for its remote genus, namely body which is above inanimate, is susceptible to voluntary motion, not like the absence of subsistence in another for the separate [intellect], for subsistence in another is not of the nature of the separate, nor of its type or genus at all, since substance was not made a genus for it, so it is the real privation and possession. For the real of privation and possession, the impossibility of coexistence relative to the capable subject is not sufficient, rather it is necessary that the relation to it be taken into account in the concept of the privative.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'adam wa malaka (privation and possession) and divides it into two types: common (mashhooriyan) and real (haqiqiyan). The author begins by stating the general principle: the two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one essence. Then he specifies the first kind: privation and possession. This occurs when one thing is the absence of a quality that the subject is naturally capable of possessing at a given time. The example given is the 'kawsaj' (sparsely bearded man) — he lacks a full beard, but his nature is to have one at that time, unlike a beardless youth (amrad) who lacks beard naturally. This is the common type. The real type occurs when the capacity for the quality is considered more broadly, either based on the type of the subject (e.g., blindness in a person born blind, as humans are capable of sight) or based on the proximate or remote genus (e.g., blindness in a scorpion, which belongs to the genus 'animal' capable of sight, or lack of motion in a mountain, which belongs to the genus 'body' capable of voluntary motion). However, lack of subsistence in another for a separate intellect is not real privation, because subsistence in another is not part of its nature or its genus. Finally, the author emphasizes that for real privation and possession, it is not enough that the two cannot coexist relative to a capable subject; rather, the relation to that subject must be inherent in the concept of the privative term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think any two things that cannot coexist are 'adam wa malaka.
Correction: Privation and possession requires that the subject be capable of possessing the quality; the impossibility of coexistence alone is not sufficient, especially for the real type where the relation to the subject must be part of the privative concept.

Checkpoint
Why is the absence of voluntary motion in a mountain considered a real privation (haqiqiyan)?
A) Because a mountain cannot move voluntarily at any time.
B) Because a mountain's remote genus (body) is capable of voluntary motion.
C) Because voluntary motion is the opposite of rest.
D) Because the mountain is an inanimate object.

Key Takeaway
The real type of privation and possession requires that the privative term inherently includes a relation to a subject that is capable of the opposite quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moral or spiritual quality you believe you lack—like patience, humility, or sincerity. According to this lesson, is that lack a real privation? That is, is your nature truly capable of that quality? Or have you convinced yourself it's not in your nature? What will you do today to test whether you truly are capable?
?
When you see someone else lacking something you think they should have—whether knowledge, piety, or good character—do you judge them fairly? The passage teaches that real privation depends on the subject's capacity. Are you presuming a capacity that may not exist? How can you more carefully assess what is truly possible for them?
?
The lesson says that for real privation, the relation to the subject must be part of the concept of the privative term. Consider a sin you struggle with. Is that struggle a privation of righteousness? If you are naturally capable of obedience, then your failure is a real privation—and you are responsible. Will you accept that responsibility and change, or will you rationalize it away?

Salb wa Ijab: Negation and Affirmation
Defines salb wa ijab as a kind of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of salb wa ijab (negation and affirmation) as a kind of opposition and how it differs from privation and possession.

Core Idea
Negation and affirmation is opposition without a receiver.

The philosophers identify several kinds of opposition. One key kind is negation and affirmation, which applies when we do not consider a subject capable of receiving the quality. This lesson explains that distinction and its relation to the previous discussion of privation and possession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a remark about possession, which sets the context.
2
Understand the condition for salb wa ijab
The passage states that if the relation to a receiver is not considered, the pair is negation and affirmation. Identify this condition.
The example given is 'man' and 'not-man'.
3
Compare with privation and possession
Recall that privation and possession require a subject capable of the quality. Here, negation and affirmation does not. Reflect on the difference.
The previous discussion on malaka helps clarify this.
4
Reflect on the logical nature
Ask yourself: Why is 'man' and 'not-man' a logical opposition rather than a real one? This will deepen your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والملكة أعم من المشهوري منهما، على عكس الحقيقي والمشهوري في المتضادين. وإن لم يعتبر ذلك من نسبة المتقابلين إلى قابل للأمر الوجودي، فسلب وإيجاب، نحو الإنسان واللاإنسان. ثم إن هاهنا مباحث الأول: قالت الحكماء: كل اثنين إن اشتركا في تمام الماهية فهما المثلان، وإن لم يشتركا فيه فهما المتخالفان، وقسموا المتخالفين إلى المتقابلين وغيرهما، وعرفوا المتقابلين بما مر. واعتبر بعضهم في تعريفهما الموضوع بدل الذات، وأرادوا به المحل المستغني عما يحل فيه، ولذلك صرحوا بأن لا تضاد في الجواهر؛ إذ لا موضوع لها. واعتبر آخرون المحل مطلقاً، ولذلك أثبتوا التضاد بين الصور النوعية للعناصر. ويظهر من ذلك أن المراد بامتناع اجتماعهما في ذات واحدة امتناع اجتماعهما بحسب الحلول فيه، لا بحسب الصدق والحمل عليه، فإن امتناع الاجتماع من حيث الصدق قد يسمى تقابلاً آخر.
And possession is more general than the common one of the two, contrary to the real and common in the two contradictories. And if that is not considered from the relation of the two opposites to a receiver of the existent thing, then it is negation and affirmation, such as man and not-man. Then there are here investigations: the first: The philosophers said: Every two that share in the complete essence are two likes, and if they do not share in it, then they are two different ones. And they divided the different ones into opposites and others. And they defined opposites as was mentioned. And some considered in their definition the subject instead of the essence, intending by it the locus that is independent of what inheres in it, and therefore they explicitly stated that there is no opposition in substances, since they have no subject. And others considered the locus absolutely, and therefore they affirmed opposition between the specific forms of the elements. And it appears from that that what is meant by the impossibility of their coexistence in one essence is the impossibility of their coexistence in terms of inherence in it, not in terms of predication and attribution, for the impossibility of coexistence in terms of predication may be called another kind of opposition.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage discusses the third kind of opposition, salb wa ijab (negation and affirmation), and then presents the philosophers' broader classification of all pairs. The author first clarifies that 'possession' (malaka) is more general than the common type of privation and possession, and then states that if the relation to a receiver of the existent thing is not considered, the pair is negation and affirmation, exemplified by 'man' and 'not-man'. Next, he reports the philosophers' division: any two things that share the complete essence are 'likes' (mithlan), otherwise they are 'different ones' (mutakhālifān). The different ones are divided into opposites (mutaqābilān) and others. The definition of opposites is as given earlier. The passage then notes a disagreement: some define opposites in terms of 'subject' (subjectum) rather than 'essence', meaning a locus independent of what inheres in it, which leads them to deny opposition in substances (since substances have no subject). Others take 'locus' absolutely and thus affirm opposition between the specific forms of the elements. The author concludes that the impossibility of coexistence in one essence refers to impossibility in terms of inherence, not in terms of predication; the latter may be called another kind of opposition.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think negation and affirmation is the same as privation and possession.
Correction: Negation and affirmation does not require a subject capable of receiving the quality; it is purely logical.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a pair to be classified as salb wa ijab?
A) The two items share the complete essence.
B) The relation to a receiver of the existent thing is not considered.
C) They are opposites in the real sense.
D) They are both substances.

Key Takeaway
Negation and affirmation is opposition without a receiver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm 'La ilaha illa Allah.' According to this lesson, the negation 'لا إله' and affirmation 'إلا الله' form a salb wa ijab with no receiver. Does your heart treat other 'gods'—like status or wealth—as possible receivers, or do you truly negate without reservation?
?
When you say 'I am not a disbeliever,' is that merely a logical negation, or does your heart actively affirm iman? The lesson shows that salb and ijab is opposition without a receiver. Are you negating disbelief while your heart still harbors attachment to worldly idols?
