Philosophers' Classification of Pairs: Likes, Different, and Opposites
Presents the philosophers' classification and the subject/essence debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of all pairs into likes, different ones, and opposites, and the debate about using subject or essence in defining opposites.

Core Idea
Pairs are first divided by essence sharing; opposites are a subset of different ones.

The philosophers provide a systematic way of categorizing any two things. They start by checking if the two share the complete essence—if yes, they are likes; if not, they are different. Different ones are further divided into opposites and others. This lesson unpacks that classification and the ensuing debate about the definition of opposites, particularly regarding the role of a subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with 'Then there are here investigations: the first'.
2
Understand the primary division
The philosophers divide all pairs into likes (share complete essence) and different (do not share). Identify this step and the criterion.
3
Learn the subdivision of different ones
Different ones are subdivided into opposites and non-opposites. Note that opposites are defined as before.
This shows that not all different things are opposites.
4
Examine the debate on definition
Some define opposites using 'subject' (locus independent of inherence) and others use 'locus' absolutely. See how this affects views on substances and elemental forms.
This debate arises from interpreting the definition of opposites.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author clarifies that the impossibility of coexistence refers to inherence, not predication. The latter is a different kind of opposition.
This nuance avoids confusion.

Correlatives and Their Subject Condition
Defines correlatives, condition of common subject, and classification into similar/dissimilar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of correlatives, the role of a common subject, and the classification of things into similars and dissimilars.

Core Idea
Correlatives are two things that cannot coexist in the same subject.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the foundational concept of correlatives (mutqabilān) in logic. It explains not only their definition but also the critical condition of a common subject that distinguishes genuine correlatives from mere logical opposition. The philosophers' classification of similars and dissimilars is also presented, along with a nuanced discussion about the nature of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and condition
Start with the core definition: correlatives are two things that do not coexist. Notice the immediate mention of a common subject as a condition.
The author stresses that without a common subject for an existential thing, the correlatives reduce to pure negation and affirmation.
2
Understand the exception
The author gives an example: if the condition of a receptive subject is not considered, then the correlatives become like 'human' and 'not-human', which is just negation and affirmation.
3
Grasp the classification of similars and dissimilars
The passage presents the philosophers' division: two things that share completely in quiddity are similars (mithlān); those that do not are dissimilars (mukhtalifān). Dissimilars are further divided into correlatives and others.
This classification is a key logical tool.
4
Examine the debate about the subject
Some scholars require the subject to be an independent locus (maḥall) for inherence, leading them to deny opposition in substances. Others allow any locus, affirming opposition between elemental forms.
This shows how the definition's precise wording affects ontological commitments.
5
Clarify the meaning of 'impossibility of coexistence'
The author explains that the impossibility refers to coexistence by inherence (ḥulūl), not by predication (ḥaml). The latter is merely disparity (tabāyun).
This final point prevents confusion with mere logical contrariety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان. والملكة أعم من المشهوري منهما، على عكس الحقيقي والمشهوري في المتضادين. (وإن لم يعتبر ذلك) الذي ذكرناه من نسبة المتقابلين إلى قابل للأمر الوجودي، (فسلب وإيجاب، نحو الإنسان واللاإنسان). ثم إن هاهنا مباحث الأول: قالت الحكماء: كل اثنين إن اشتركا في تمام الماهية فهما المثلان، وإن لم يشتركا فيه فهما المتخالفان، وقسموا المتخالفين إلى المتقابلين، وغيرهما، وعرفوا المتقابلين بما مر واعتبر بعضهم في تعريفهما الموضوع بدل الذات، وأرادوا به المحل المستغني عما يحل فيه، ولذلك صرحوا بأن لا تضاد في الجواهر؛ إذ لا موضوع لها. واعتبر آخرون المحل مطلقاً، ولذلك أثبتوا التضاد بين الصور النوعية للعناصر، ويظهر ذلك أن المراد بامتناع اجتماعهما في ذات واحدة امتناع اجتماعهما بحسب الحلول فيه، لا بحسب الصدق والحمل عليه فإن امتناع الاجتماع من حيث الصدق قد يسمى تبايناً.
The two correlatives (al-mutaqabilān) are two things that do not coexist (lā yajtami‘ān). And possession (al-malaka) is more general than the well-known (al-mashhūrī) of the two, unlike the real (al-ḥaqīqī) and the well-known in opposites (al-mutaḍāddīn). (And if that is not considered) that which we mentioned of the relation of correlatives to a receptive subject for the existential thing, then it is negation and affirmation (salb wa ījāb), like 'human' and 'not-human'. Then there are here investigations. The first: The philosophers said: Every two things, if they share in the complete quiddity, then they are similars (al-mithlān), and if they do not share in it, then they are dissimilars (al-mukhtalifān). And they divided the dissimilars into the correlatives and others, and defined the correlatives as already mentioned. And some considered in their definition the subject (al-mawḍū‘) instead of the essence (al-dhāt), and by it they meant the locus (al-maḥall) independent of what inheres in it, and therefore they stated that there is no opposition (taḍādd) in substances because they have no subject. And others considered the locus absolutely, and therefore they affirmed opposition between the specific forms of the elements. And it appears by that that what is meant by the impossibility of their coexistence in a single essence is the impossibility of their coexistence with respect to inherence (al-ḥulūl) in it, not with respect to truth (al-ṣidq) and predication (al-ḥaml) upon it, for the impossibility of coexistence with respect to truth may be called disparity (tabāyun).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the logical notion of 'correlatives' (mutaqabilan) as two things that cannot coexist. It begins by stating this definition and then discusses the condition of a common subject. The author notes that possession (malaka) is more general than the well-known type of correlative, contrasting with the situation in opposites. He then introduces an exception: if the condition of a common subject for an existential thing is not considered, then the correlatives reduce to pure negation and affirmation, like 'human' and 'not-human'. The passage then moves to a classification from the philosophers: any two things that share completely in quiddity are 'similars' (mithlan), and those that do not are 'dissimilars' (mukhtalifan). Dissimilars are then divided into correlatives and others. A key point is the role of the subject (mawdu‘) in the definition of correlatives. Some scholars insist that the subject must be a locus independent of what inheres in it, leading them to deny the possibility of opposition in substances (since substances have no subject). Others allow any locus, and thus they affirm opposition between the specific forms of elements. The author clarifies that the impossibility of coexistence in a single essence refers to coexistence by inherence, not by predication; the latter is merely disparity. The passage explains the technical conditions that separate genuine correlatives from mere logical contrariety.

الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two concepts that cannot both be true of the same thing are correlatives.
Correction: The passage shows that genuine correlatives require a common subject for inherence; otherwise they are merely negation and affirmation or disparity.

Checkpoint
Why do some philosophers deny the existence of opposition (tadadd) in substances?
A) Because substances have no subject for inherence.
B) Because substances are simple and cannot be opposites.
C) Because substances are eternal and unchanging.
D) Because substances are not real.

Key Takeaway
Correlatives require a common subject for inherence; without it, they are not true correlatives but mere logical opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in tawḥīd, yet do your actions sometimes contradict that belief? What is the 'subject' in which these contradictions inhere—your soul, your habits, your priorities? How can you ensure that only the implications of tawḥīd fully inhere in your heart, without allowing any opposing allegiance to coexist? Today, identify one concrete attachment that competes with your devotion to Allah and begin the process of removing it.
?
The scholars debated whether substances can have opposition. Reflect on your own 'substance'—your innate nature (fiṭrah). Do you find that some vices seem to oppose your fiṭrah, yet you still commit them? What does that say about the locus of those vices? Are they merely predicated on you (superficial), or do they inhere in your soul (deep-rooted)? Make a firm intention to uproot one vice that has taken residence, seeking Allah's help.
?
In the passage, correlatives require a common subject for inherence. In your relationship with Allah, do you try to combine love of this world and love of Allah in the same heart? Can they genuinely coexist? Examine your heart today: which love dominates? If worldly love inheres, repent and strive to make love of Allah the sole inhering quality in your soul.

The Fourfold Division of Correlatives
Enumerates the four kinds of correlatives with their criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of correlatives and the criteria used to distinguish them.

Core Idea
Correlatives are divided into four types based on their existential status and relation.

This lesson introduces the classical division of correlatives (al-mutaqābilūn) into four kinds. The division is based on two primary distinctions: whether both are existential or only one, and whether they are mutually relational. Understanding this classification is essential for later theological arguments about attributes and relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the division passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It begins with the well-known division and ends with the fourth type.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the conditional clauses.
2
Identify the four types
List the four kinds: correlates, opposites, privation & possession, negation & affirmation. Note the defining condition for each.
3
Understand the decision criteria
Notice that the first split is whether both are existential. Then for both existential, whether they are conceived in relation to each other. For one existential one non-existential, whether the non-existential has a receptive subject.
This creates a binary tree of distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور في تقسيم المتقابلين أنهما إما وجوديان أو لا، وعلى الأول إما أن يكون تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، فهما المتضايفان أو لا، فهما المتضادان. وعلى الثاني يكون أحدهما وجودياً، والآخر عدمياً، فإما أن يعتبر في العدمي محل قابل للوجودي، فهما العدم والملكة أو لا فهما السلب والإيجاب. واعترض عليه أولاً بجواز كونهما عدميين كالعمى واللاعمى. وأجيب: بأن العدم المطلق لا يقابل نفسه، ولا العدم المضاف؛ لاجتماعه معه، والعدم المضاف لا يقابل العدم المضاف لاجتماعهما في كل موجود مغاير لما أضيف إليه العدمان، وأما العمى فهو
The well-known [division] in the division of correlatives is that they are either both existential (wujūdiyān) or not. In the first case, either each of them is conceived in relation to the other, then they are the correlates (al-mutadāyifān), or not, then they are the opposites (al-mutadāddān). In the second case, one of them is existential and the other is non-existential. Then, if in the non-existential a subject receptive to the existential is considered, then they are privation and possession (al-‘adm wa al-malaka), or if not, then they are negation and affirmation (al-salb wa al-ījāb). And it was objected against this first: by the possibility that both are non-existential, like blindness and non-blindness. And it was answered: that absolute non-existence does not oppose itself, nor does relative non-existence (al-‘adm al-muḍāf) oppose it, due to its coexistence with it; and relative non-existence does not oppose relative non-existence due to their coexistence in every existent other than that to which the two non-existences are related. As for blindness, it is...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the standard fourfold division of correlatives. It states that correlatives are either both existential (wujūdiyan) or not. If both are existential, they are further divided: if each is conceived in relation to the other, they are 'correlates' (mutadayifan), like father and son; if not, they are 'opposites' (mutadaddan), like black and white. If one is existential and the other non-existential, then there are two possibilities: if the non-existential requires a subject capable of receiving the existential, they are 'privation and possession' (al-‘adm wa al-malaka), e.g., blindness and sight; if no such subject is required, they are 'negation and affirmation' (al-salb wa al-ījab), e.g., 'human' and 'not-human'. The passage then records a first objection: why cannot both correlatives be non-existential, such as blindness and non-blindness? The answer begins by explaining that absolute non-existence cannot oppose itself, nor can relative non-existence oppose absolute non-existence because they would coexist. Similarly, two relative non-existences cannot oppose each other because they would coexist in any existent unrelated to their respective subjects. The answer continues but is cut off at the page break.

Key Terms
وجوديان
عدميان
المتضايفان
المتضادان
العدم والملكة
السلب والإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'privation and possession' and 'negation and affirmation' are the same thing.
Correction: They differ based on whether the non-existential term requires a receptive subject for the existential. Privation (e.g., blindness) implies a subject capable of sight, while negation (e.g., non-human) does not.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes 'opposites' from 'correlates'?
A) Correlates are both existential, opposites are not.
B) Correlates are conceived in relation to each other, opposites are not.
C) Opposites are both existential, correlates are not.
D) Correlates require a receptive subject.

Key Takeaway
The fourfold division of correlatives is based on existential status and the condition of mutual relation or receptive subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you viewed someone purely as an 'opposite' (competitor, enemy). Could you instead see them as a 'correlative' (someone whose existence defines a role for you)? Write down one practical step to transform that relationship from opposition to mutual definition.
?
Consider a personal deficiency you often label as 'I am not patient' or 'I am not generous'. According to the passage, is this deficiency a mere negation (salb) or a privation ('adm) that assumes you have the capacity? If it's privation, why are you not taking ownership of acquiring that trait? Commit to one concrete action this week to develop it.
?
The passage distinguishes between correlatives that are purely existential (like father–son) and those that mix existence and non-existence (like blindness–sight). In your own life, do you over-identify with what you lack (non-existence) rather than what you have (existence)? How would reframing your deficits as privations (requiring a subject) shift your responsibility? Name one deficit you will now treat as a privation and seek to fill.

First Objection: Can Both Be Non-Existential?
Presents the first objection and part of its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that both correlatives could be non-existential and the beginning of the reply involving absolute and relative non-existence.

Core Idea
Not all correlatives are existential; the answer distinguishes absolute from relative non-existence.

After presenting the division, an objection is raised: why can't both correlatives be non-existential, like blindness and non-blindness? The answer begins by distinguishing absolute non-existence from relative non-existence and showing why they cannot oppose each other. This lesson examines the objection and the initial part of the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the objection: it states that both could be non-existential, citing blindness and non-blindness as an example.
This challenges the division's assumption that one must be existential.
2
Read the answer's first part
The answer says absolute non-existence does not oppose itself, nor does relative non-existence oppose absolute non-existence because they coexist. Then it says two relative non-existences do not oppose each other because they coexist in any existent unrelated to their subjects.
The answer continues beyond the excerpt.
3
Understand the logic
The key is that opposition requires a mutual relation of contrast, which non-existences cannot fulfill because they can coexist in the same thing.

Opposition by Contrariety
Defines contrariety with example and condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of contrariety (tadadd) and its requirement that contraries exist externally and cannot coexist in the same substrate at the same time.

Core Idea
Contraries are real external qualities that exclude each other in the same subject.

In logic, things can oppose each other in different ways. One fundamental type is contrariety, where two qualities cannot be together in the same thing at once. This lesson explains exactly what contrariety means according to the classical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the condition 'with respect to a single substrate at a single time'.
This is the key clause that distinguishes contrariety from other oppositions.
2
Identify the example
Note that blackness and whiteness are given as paradigmatic contraries. Ask yourself why they cannot coexist in the same subject.
3
Understand the existential requirement
The passage stresses that contraries exist externally, not merely in the mind. This means they are real properties of things.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
The rule 'if one exists, the other is prevented' is a core logical principle. Think of examples from everyday life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقابلان تقابل التضاد كالسواد والبياض يتقابلان باعتبار وجودهما في الخارج مقيساً إلى محل واحد في زمان واحد، فإذا وجد فيه أحدهما امتنع به وجود الآخر، فالمتضادان المذكوران أمران موجودان في الخارج. وكذلك المتقابلان تقابل التضايف كالأبوة والبنوة يتقابلان باعتبار وجودهما في الخارج في محل واحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة على مذهب من قال بوجود الإضافات في الخارج. وأما على مذهب من قال بعدمها مطلقاً، فالتقابل بينهما باعتبار اتصاف المحل بهما في الخارج.
The two correlatives in the opposition of contrariety, like blackness and whiteness, are opposed by virtue of their existence in the external [world] measured with respect to a single substrate at a single time; when one of them exists in it, the existence of the other is prevented. Thus the two mentioned contraries are two things existing externally. Similarly, the two correlatives in the opposition of correlation, like fatherhood and sonship, are opposed by virtue of their existence in the external [world] in a single substrate at a single time from one aspect, according to the doctrine of those who affirm the existence of relations externally. But according to the doctrine of those who deny them absolutely, the opposition between them is by virtue of the substrate's being qualified by them externally.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines two types of opposition: contrariety (tadadd) and correlation (tadayuf). It begins by stating that contraries, such as black and white, are opposed through their external existence; they cannot coexist in the same substrate at the same time. The passage emphasizes that contraries are existent things in the external world. Then it turns to correlation, like father and son, which are also opposed through external existence according to those who consider relations as real external entities. However, for those who deny external relations, the opposition is instead based on the substrate's being characterized by these relational attributes externally.

Key Terms
تقابل التضاد
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contraries are merely logical opposites with no real external existence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that contraries are 'things existing externally', so they are real properties.

Checkpoint
What is the condition given for two things to be contraries?
A) They exist in the mind at the same time
B) They exist externally in the same substrate at the same time mutually exclusive
C) They are relative to each other
D) They are both in the same category

Key Takeaway
Contrariety requires two real external qualities that cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that contraries like blackness and whiteness cannot coexist in the same substrate at the same time. How can you ensure that only one dominates your soul?
?
If there were two gods, they would be contraries and could not both be omnipotent. Have you truly internalised the logical necessity of tawhid? Reflect on any moment where you acted as if something else shared Allah's power. How can you uproot that from your heart?

Opposition by Correlation
Defines correlation with example and two metaphysical views.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Correlative opposites depend on each other, but their reality is debated.

Another type of opposition is correlation, where two things are defined in relation to each other, like father and son. This lesson presents the definition and notes a key philosophical disagreement about whether such relations exist outside the mind.

Guided Path

Read the definition for correlation

2
Understand the example

A person cannot be both father and son to the same person.
3
Learn the two doctrines
The passage presents two views: one affirms relations exist externally, the other denies them entirely. Note how the definition changes accordingly.
4
Compare with contrariety

Opposition by Privation and Possession
Defines the opposition of privation and possession with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of opposition by privation and possession, and how it differs from other types of opposition.

Core Idea
Privation is not a thing; it's the absence of a possession in a subject.

This passage from al-Sanusi introduces a specific type of opposition used in logic and theology. It clarifies that in this opposition, one correlative (possession) exists externally in a subject, while the other (privation) is merely its absence. This distinction is important for understanding how we talk about attributes and their opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the basic definition of opposition by privation and possession.
Pay attention to the example of sight and blindness.
2
Identify the two correlatives
Notice that possession (like sight) is said to exist externally, while privation (blindness) is the absence of that possession in the subject.
The opposition arises from the subject being qualified by either possession or its absence.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that privation is an external entity. The passage clarifies that privation is not something that exists externally; it is merely the lack of possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتقابلان تقابل العدم والملكة يكون أحدهما أعني الملكة كالبصر موجوداً خارجياً، فهو بحسب هذا الوجود في المحل يقابل العمى بحسب اتصاف المحل به.
And the two correlatives in the opposition of privation and possession: one of them, namely possession, like sight, is existent externally; thus, according to this existence in the substrate, it is opposed to blindness according to the substrate's being qualified by it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the opposition of privation (adam) and possession (mulka) by giving an example: sight (possession) exists externally in a subject, and it is opposed to blindness (privation) not as an external entity but as the subject's lack of sight. The opposition is understood in terms of the subject being characterized by either possession or its absence.

The Mental Nature of Affirmation and Negation
Defines affirmation and negation as mental, not external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the opposition of affirmation and negation is a mental judgment, not an external reality.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation exist only in the mind or in speech, not in the external world.

This passage from al-Mawaqif clarifies the nature of the opposition between affirmation and negation. It emphasizes that these are mental constructs applied to relations, which are also mental. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later discussions about the logical relations between propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: إيجاب (affirmation), سلب (negation), نسبة (relation), أمر عقلي (mental matter).
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that affirmation and negation have no external existence. Identify why: because they are mental matters occurring to a mental relation.
Contrast this with the previous types of opposition (e.g., possession and privation) which have external existence.
3
Distinguish real vs. metaphorical existence
The passage says affirmation and negation exist in the mind (real existence) or in speech (metaphorical existence). Understand what this means: the actual judgment is mental, its expression is verbal.
This distinction is important for later discussions on logic and language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإيجاب والسلب، فهما أمران عقليان واردان على النسبة التي هي عقلية أيضاً، فلا وجود للمتقابلين هاهنا في الخارج أصلاً؛ لأن ثبوت النسبة وانتفاءها ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية، بل من الأمور الذهنية، فإذا حصلا في العقل كان كل منهما عقداً أي اعتقاداً. فالمتقابلان هاهنا يوجدان في الذهن، وهو وجود حقيقي لهما، أو في القول إذا عبر عنهما بعبارة وهو وجود مجازي، وهذا معنى ما قيل من أن تقابل الإيجاب والسلب راجع إلى القول والعقد.
As for affirmation and negation, they are two mental matters that occur to the relation, which is also mental. So there is no existence for the two correlatives here in the external [world] at all, because the affirmation and negation of the relation are not among external existents, but rather mental matters. When they occur in the intellect, each of them is a belief, i.e., a conviction. Thus the two correlatives here exist in the mind, which is a real existence for them, or in speech when they are expressed in words, which is a metaphorical existence. And this is the meaning of what has been said: that the opposition of affirmation and negation goes back to speech and belief.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the opposition of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salab). It begins by stating that affirmation and negation are mental entities that apply to a relation, which is also mental. Therefore, they have no external existence, as the presence or absence of a relation is not an external object but a mental construct. When they are in the intellect, each constitutes a belief. They exist genuinely in the mind, or metaphorically in speech. The author then connects this to the saying that opposition of affirmation and negation reduces to speech and belief, emphasizing their non-external nature.

Correction: The passage clarifies that they are mental judgments (beliefs) about relations, which are also mental, and have no external existence.

The opposition of affirmation and negation is a mental relation, not a physical one; they exist in the mind or speech, not externally.

The Fourth Type of Opposition: Horse and Non-Horse
Analyzes the opposition between 'horse' and 'non-horse' in two scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways the opposition between the concepts of horse and non-horse can be analyzed, depending on whether truth is considered.

Core Idea
The opposition of simple concepts depends on whether truth is attached.

This passage from al-Mawaqif continues the classification of types of opposition. Here, the author examines the opposition between a simple concept and its negation. The analysis reveals that this opposition is not uniform; it changes fundamentally depending on whether we consider the concept's truth applying to something. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The passage considers the concept of 'horse' and its opposite 'non-horse'. Recognize that this is a simple concept pair.
2
First case: truth considered
If we consider the truth of 'horse' applying to something, then 'non-horse' negates that truth. This yields two subcases: declarative or restrictive relation.
3
Second case: no truth considered
If we do not consider any truth-relation, then 'non-horse' is simply the concept of 'not' attached to 'horse', and there is no real negation.
4
Reflect on the difference
Notice that in the first case there is a real opposition (potentially two propositions), while in the second case there is no negation in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إذا اعتبر مفهوم الفرس، فإن اعتبر معه صدقه على شيء، فيكون اللافرس سلباً لذلك الصدق، وحينئذ إما أن تكون النسبة بالصدق خبرية، فهما في المعنى قضيتان بالفعل أو تقييدية، فلا تقابل بينهما إلا باعتبار وقوع تلك النسبة إيجاباً ولا وقوعها سلباً، فيرجعان بالقوة إلى قضيتين. وإذا اعتبر مفهوم الفرس ولم يلاحظ معه نسبة بالصدق على شيء يكون مفهوم اللافرس حينئذ هو مفهوم كلمة لا مقيداً بمفهوم الفرس، ولا سلب في الحقيقة هاهنا.
The fourth: if the concept of horse is considered, then if its truth of something is considered along with it, then non-horse is a negation of that truth. In that case, either the relation by truth is declarative, so they are in meaning two actual propositions, or restrictive, so there is no opposition between them except by virtue of the occurrence of that relation as affirmation or its non-occurrence as negation, so they revert potentially to two propositions. If the concept of horse is considered and there is no relation by truth to anything observed with it, then the concept of non-horse in that case is the concept of the word 'not' restricted by the concept of horse, and there is no negation in reality here.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth type of opposition, which involves simple concepts like 'horse' and its opposite 'non-horse'. It distinguishes two cases: (1) When the concept of horse is considered together with its truth applying to something, then 'non-horse' negates that truth. If the relation is declarative (khabariyya), they are effectively two propositions; if restrictive (taqyidiyya), their opposition is only in terms of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation, potentially reducing to two propositions. (2) When the concept of horse is considered without any truth-relation to something, then 'non-horse' is simply the concept of the word 'not' attached to 'horse', and there is no real negation involved.

Key Terms
مفهوم الفرس
اللافرس
صدق
قضية خبرية
قضية تقييدية
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All oppositions of simple concepts are the same regardless of context.
Correction: The passage shows that the opposition between a concept and its negation differs fundamentally depending on whether truth is considered. When truth is involved, there is real opposition; when it is not, there is no negation in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is 'non-horse' when the concept of horse is considered without any truth-relation?
A) A negation of a proposition
B) The concept of 'not' restricted by the concept of horse
C) A privation
D) A contrary

Key Takeaway
The opposition between a concept and its negation is not uniform; it depends on whether truth is considered, with real negation only occurring when truth is attached.

The Locus of Opposition Between Affirmation and Negation
Defines the opposition between affirmation and negation as located in mind or speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the opposition between affirmation and negation exists in the mind or in speech, not in external reality, and that it concerns the judgment of a relation.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation are mental, not external.

This passage explains where logical opposites like affirmation and negation actually reside. You might think they are out in the world, but the author argues they are entirely mental or linguistic. Let's see how he makes this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its flow, then read the translation. Notice the key terms: إيجاب, سلب, نسبة.
The passage includes both the original matn and a commentary; focus on the matn first.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that the two opposites have no existence in the external world. Why? Because affirmation and negation are about relations, which are mental.
Look for the phrase 'لا يجتمعان وجود للمتقابلين هاهنا في الخارج أصلاً'.
3
Understand the two loci
The passage says opposites exist either in the mind (real existence) or in speech (metaphorical existence). Distinguish between these two types of existence.
Real existence in the mind means the judgment itself; metaphorical existence is when it is expressed.
4
Examine the correction of a misunderstanding
The commentator refutes an interpretation from Sharh al-Tajrid that would treat the opposition as between two perceptions. Instead, it is about the same relation being affirmed or denied.
This is clarified in the commentary after 'قوله : (فإذا حصلا في العقل...'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان
وجود للمتقابلين هاهنا في الخارج أصلاً؛ لأن ثبوت النسبة، وانتفاءها ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية، بل من الأمور الذهنية، فإذا حصلا في العقل كان كل منهما عقداً أي اعتقاداً. فالمتقابلان هاهنا يوجدان في الذهن، وهو وجود حقيقي لهما، أو في القول إذا عبر عنهما بعبارة وهو وجود مجازي، وهذا معنى ما قيل من أن تقابل الإيجاب والسلب راجع إلى القول والعقد .
قوله : (فإذا حصلا في العقل هذا صريح في أن المراد بالإيجاب والسلب والوقوع واللاوقوع، فما في شرح التجريد من أن الشارح قدس سره اعتبر التقابل بين الإيجاب والسلب بمعنى الإدراكين وهم . قوله : ( كان كل منهما إلخ) أي الثبوت واللاثبوت عقداً؛ لأن المراد بحصولهما في العقل الإذعان بأن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة . قوله : ( فالمتقابلان ) أي الثبوت والانتفاء. أشباحها . قوله: (وهو وجود حقيقي لهما بناء على أن الحاصل في الذهن ماهيات الأشياء لا [؟] . قوله : ( وهذا معنى ما قيل إلخ) أي إن المتقابلين هاهنا موجودان في الذهن، لا أن تقابلهما باعتبار الوجود في الذهن وقيامهما به فإنه تقابل التضاد فعلى تحقيق الشارح قدس سره تكون النسبة مورداً للإيجاب والسلب، بمعنى أنه يمتنع اتصاف النسبة الحكمية المخصوصة بهما في الذهن في زمان واحد.
Fourth Observatory - Eleventh Intent: The two opposites are two things that do not come together. They have no existence for the two opposites here in the external world at all, because the affirmation of the relation and its negation are not among external existents, but rather among mental matters. So when they occur in the mind, each of them is a belief, i.e., an assent. So the two opposites here exist in the mind, which is a real existence for them, or in speech if they are expressed by an expression, which is a metaphorical existence. This is the meaning of what was said: that the opposition of affirmation and negation reduces to speech and belief.
His saying: (When they occur in the mind) This is clear that what is meant by affirmation and negation is occurrence and non-occurrence. So what is in Sharh al-Tajrid that the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) considered the opposition between affirmation and negation in the sense of two perceptions is an illusion. His saying: (each of them is [a belief] etc.) means that affirmation and non-affirmation are a belief, because what is meant by their occurrence in the mind is the acknowledgement that the relation occurs or does not occur. His saying: (the two opposites) means affirmation and negation; their forms. His saying: (and it is a real existence for them) based on the fact that what occurs in the mind are the quiddities of things, not [?]. His saying: (and this is the meaning of what was said etc.) means that the two opposites here exist in the mind, not that their opposition is by virtue of existence in the mind and their subsistence in it, for that would be opposition of contraries. So according to the verification of the commentator (may his soul be sanctified), the relation is the subject of affirmation and negation, meaning that it is impossible for the specific judgmental relation to be qualified by both of them in the mind at one time.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of opposition between affirmation and negation, and its locus. The original text (matn) states that the two opposites (here referring to affirmation and negation) have no existence in the external world; rather, they are mental matters. When they occur in the mind, each is a belief or assent. Their existence is either real (in the mind) or metaphorical (in speech). The commentary clarifies that 'occurrence in the mind' means the acknowledgement that a relation does or does not hold. The commentator refutes a misunderstanding from Sharh al-Tajrid that would take the opposition as between two perceptions. Instead, the opposition is between the affirmation and negation of a relational judgment. The existence in the mind is real because mental contents are the quiddities of things, not their external forms. The commentator emphasizes that the opposition here is not like the opposition of contraries (which requires two distinct existences in a subject), but rather the impossibility of the same judgmental relation being both affirmed and denied at the same time. The key is that the relation itself is the subject of the two opposite qualifications.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opposition between affirmation and negation is between two perceptions or external things.
Correction: The opposition is between the affirmation and negation of a single judgmental relation, which exists either in the mind or in speech.

Checkpoint

B) In the mind or in speech

Key Takeaway
The opposition of affirmation and negation is fundamentally about mental judgments of a relation, not about external entities.

Opposition of Simple Concepts: Horse and Non-Horse
Analyzes the opposition between simple concepts like horse and non-horse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how simple concepts like 'horse' and 'non-horse' can be considered as logical opposites in two distinct ways: when they involve a truth relation (leading to propositions) and when they are considered without any relation (leading to mutual exclusion).

Core Idea
Even simple concepts can be opposites by mutual exclusion, not just as propositions.

This passage from the logical section examines the opposition of simple concepts, using 'horse' and 'non-horse' as a key example. It distinguishes two scenarios: one where the concept is considered together with a truth relation (which yields potential propositions), and another where it is considered alone (where the opposite is merely the negation of the concept). The discussion also addresses objections about the nature of negation and the locus of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two main divisions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the distinction between considering 'horse' with a relation of truth and without it.
2
Understand the first division: concept with truth relation
Under this division, 'non-horse' negates the truth of 'horse' on something. This reduces to either actual propositions or potential propositions.
Notice how this yields propositional opposites.
3
Understand the second division: concept without relation
Here, 'non-horse' is just the concept 'not' tied to 'horse', and there is no real negation. Yet the two are still opposites by mutual exclusion.
4
Examine the objections and responses
The passage presents objections that negation requires a relation and that opposition requires a locus. The responses clarify that mutual exclusion itself constitutes opposition.
Pay attention to the example of 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إذا اعتبر مفهوم الفرس، فإن اعتبر معه صدقه على شيء، فيكون اللافرس سلباً لذلك الصدق، وحينئذ إما أن تكون النسبة بالصدق خبرية، فهما في المعنى قضيتان بالفعل أو تقييدية، فلا تقابل بينهما إلا باعتبار وقوع تلك النسبة إيجاباً ولا وقوعها سلباً، فيرجعان بالقوة إلى قضيتين، وإذا اعتبر مفهوم الفرس، ولم يلاحظ معه نسبة بالصدق على شيء يكون مفهوم اللافرس حينئذ هو مفهوم كلمة لا مقيداً بمفهوم الفرس، ولا سلب في الحقيقة هاهنا؛
قوله : ( فلا تقابل بينهما إلخ إذ الحيوان المقيد بالناطق واللاناطق مثلاً كلاهما حاصلان معاً في الذهن والخارج.
قوله: (ولا) سلب في الحقيقة قيل فيه نظر؛ إذ حينئذ لا يرد مفهوم الفرس واللافرس، إذ لا يتصور ورود سلب أو إيجاب إلا على نسبة؛ لأنك إذا اعتبرت مفهوماً واحداً، ولم تعتبر معه نسبة إلى مفهوم آخر، ولا نسبة مفهوم آخر إليه لم يكن لك إدراك وقوع، أولا وقوع متعلق بذلك المفهوم الواحد، كما تشهد به البديهة، فمفهوما الفرس واللافرس المأخوذان على هذا الوجه متباعدان في أنفسهما غاية التباعد، ومتدافعان الصدق على ذات واحدة فهما متقابلان بهذا الاعتبار.
فإن قلت : قد المعتبر في المتقابلين هو المحل، أو الموضوع وليس لمفهومي الفرس واللافرس حلول في محل، فلا تقابل بينهما .
قلت : ينقل الكلام إلى مفهومي البياض واللابياض المأخوذين على الوجه الأخير فبينهما تقابل خارج عن الأقسام الأربعة، كما أشرنا إليه .
Fourth: If the concept of horse is considered, and if with it its truth of something is considered, then non-horse is a negation of that truth. Then either the relation with respect to truth is predicational, so they are in meaning two actual propositions; or it is restrictional, so there is no opposition between them except by considering the occurrence of that relation affirmatively or its non-occurrence negatively, so they reduce potentially to two propositions. And if the concept of horse is considered, and no relation with respect to truth to something is noted with it, then the concept of non-horse is the concept of the word 'not' qualified by the concept of horse, and there is no real negation here.
His saying: (so there is no opposition between them etc.) For example, 'animal' qualified by 'rational' and 'non-rational' both exist together in the mind and externally.
His saying: (and there is no) real negation. It is said regarding it there is consideration; for then the concept of horse and non-horse does not occur, for it is not conceivable that a negation or affirmation occurs except upon a relation; because if you consider a single concept, and you do not consider with it a relation to another concept, nor a relation of another concept to it, you do not have a perception of occurrence or non-occurrence pertaining to that single concept, as intuition testifies. So the concepts 'horse' and 'non-horse' taken in this manner are remote from each other to the utmost remoteness, and they repel each other with respect to truth upon a single essence, so they are opposites in this consideration.
If you say: The consideration in opposites is the locus or subject, and the two concepts 'horse' and 'non-horse' have no inherence in a locus, so there is no opposition between them.
I say: The discussion transfers to the two concepts 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' taken in the latter manner, for between them is an opposition outside the four divisions, as we indicated.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage discusses the opposition between simple concepts, using 'horse' and 'non-horse' as an example. The original text (matn) distinguishes two considerations. First, if the concept 'horse' is considered together with its truth of something (i.e., as part of a proposition), then 'non-horse' is the negation of that truth, and the two are potential propositions. Second, if the concept 'horse' is considered alone without any relation to another concept, then 'non-horse' is merely the concept of 'not' tied to 'horse', and there is no real negation in the logical sense. The commentary then addresses an objection: some say that there is no opposition here because negation and affirmation only apply to relations. The commentator argues that if we take the simple concepts without any relation, they are still opposites because they are mutually exclusive—one cannot be true of the same thing as the other. Then an objection is raised: opposites require a locus, but these concepts do not inhere in anything. The answer is that we can consider 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' similarly, and their opposition falls outside the four standard divisions of opposition. Thus, simple concepts like 'horse' and 'non-horse' are opposites by mutual exclusion, not by the usual logical categories.

Key Terms
فرس
لافرس
مفهوم
نسبة
صدق
قضية
سلب
إيجاب
تقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That simple concepts like 'horse' and 'non-horse' are not opposites because they lack a propositional structure or a locus.
Correction: Even without a proposition or a locus, simple concepts can be opposites by mutual exclusion, as they cannot both be true of the same thing.

Checkpoint
How are the concepts 'horse' and 'non-horse' said to be opposites when taken without any relation to something else?
A) They are contradictory propositions
B) They are mutually exclusive in truth on a single essence
C) They are contraries that can coexist
D) They are not opposites at all

Key Takeaway
Simple concepts can be logical opposites either as potential propositions (when considered with a truth relation) or as mutually exclusive terms (when considered without any relation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of two simple concepts you habitually treat as opposites, like 'success' and 'failure' or 'just' and 'unjust'. According to this lesson, are they opposites even when not attached to any specific situation? How does that absolute remoteness challenge the way you judge yourself or others when you mix them with circumstances? What would change in your life if you really believed they can never coexist in the same essence?
?
The lesson shows that 'horse' and 'non-horse' are 'remote from each other to the utmost remoteness' without any relation. Are there absolute truths in your faith—like halal and haram, truth and falsehood—that you treat as relative or dependent on context? Negation and Opposition: Whiteness vs. Non-Whiteness
Explains the status of negation in simple concepts and classifies the opposition of whiteness and non-whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that whiteness and non-whiteness are not contraries but a distinct type of opposition, and how the concept of 'real negation' affects their classification.

Core Idea
The word 'not' in 'non-whiteness' removes it from the category of contraries because negation is incompatible with their definition.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif delves into the subtle classification of opposites. The commentator examines whether 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' are contraries or belong to a different category, clarifying the role of negation. Understanding this helps refine our grasp of logical opposition in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The commentator is responding to a previous point about negation. Read the opening lines to see how he sets up the discussion.
The term 'then' refers to when no relation is considered.
2
Grasp 'real negation'
Distinguish between real and apparent negation. Real negation is the removal of affirmation, which only applies to relations. This distinction is crucial for the classification.
3
Examine the classification of opposites
The passage refers to the four divisions of opposition. Understand what contraries are: two opposites where one is not the negation of the other, and understanding each does not depend on the other.
Contraries are one of the four types.
4
Follow the argument about whiteness/non-whiteness
The commentator argues that despite appearances, whiteness and non-whiteness are not contraries because the word 'not' introduces negation, which is excluded from the concept of contraries.
This is a refutation of the author's classification.

The commentator concludes that the opposition between whiteness and non-whiteness falls outside the standard four divisions, highlighting the need for precision.

His saying: (then) i.e., when not considering its relation to anything.
His saying: (and no real negation) because it is an expression of removing affirmation, and affirmation only applies to a relation, and that is apparent, and likewise negation. He only said 'in reality' because there is a negation from it in appearance. His saying: (perception of occurrence etc.) i.e., its conception, as stated in the glosses of al-Tajrid. His saying: (as we indicated) previously by his saying: 'contrary to the two concepts whiteness and non-whiteness, for it is impossible etc.'

And there is no doubt that it is true of whiteness and non-whiteness, for example, on the assumption of the absence of real negation. And the truth is that the entry of the word 'not' into the concept of non-whiteness is sufficient to exclude whiteness and non-whiteness from the contraries, and that what is meant by the negation denied from the concept of contraries and correlatives is comprehensive; for there is no ground for making a new terminology.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the nature of negation in simple concepts and examines the classification of opposition for 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness'. The commentator starts by clarifying that 'then' refers to when no relation is considered. The term 'no real negation' is explained: negation is removal of affirmation, which applies only to a relation; apparent negation exists but not real logical negation. The commentator then discusses the author's statement that either one is not the negation of the other, or if it is, real negation is intended, and the example 'human' and 'non-human' would not fit the second division of privation. The commentator argues that if the negation is not real, then the two concepts are not one the negation of the other, and they would be contraries, thus not necessarily outside the four divisions on the author's system, but they would be outside on the famous division. Next, the commentator clarifies that 'perception of occurrence' means conception, not acknowledgement, correcting a misunderstanding that the commentator's view equates opposition to two perceptions. The commentator then refers back to the earlier indication about 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness', explaining that these two can be used as a refutation of the author, since the author would put them among contraries because neither is the negation of the other (on the assumption of no real negation). However, the correct view is that the word 'not' in 'non-whiteness' is sufficient to exclude them from contraries, because the negation denied from the concept of contraries is comprehensive, so no new terminology is needed. The passage concludes that the opposition between whiteness and non-whiteness is outside the four standard divisions, and this is an objection against the author's classification or anyone who restricts opposition to four types.

Definition of Mutaqabilan and the Role of Relation
Author defines mutaqabilan and explains the role of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mutaqabilan (two opposites) and why a relational context to a common subject is necessary for two concepts to be considered opposites.

Core Idea
Opposition requires a relation; isolated concepts are not opposites.

In this passage, the author defines mutaqabilan and explains the crucial role of relation. Without a relation to a common subject, concepts like 'horse' and 'non-horse' are merely different, not technically opposites. This establishes a foundational principle for understanding opposition in logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the author's style and structure. Notice how the definition is introduced and then explained.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the key claim
Identify the central claim: opposition requires a relation (nisba). The author argues that without relating a concept to another, you cannot perceive affirmation or negation.
This is the core insight.
3
Analyze the example
Examine the 'horse' (فرس) and 'non-horse' (لافرس) example. Notice how they are 'extremely distant' in themselves but become opposites only when considered in relation to a single essence.
The example clarifies the theoretical point.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that any two different concepts are opposites. The passage shows this is false without a shared subject of predication.
This guards against a common error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان إذ لا يتصور ورود سلب أو إيجاب إلا على نسبة؛ لأنك إذا اعتبرت مفهوماً واحداً، ولم تعتبر معه نسبة إلى مفهوم آخر، ولا نسبة مفهوم آخر إليه لم يكن لك إدراك وقوع، أولا وقوع متعلق بذلك المفهوم الواحد، كما تشهد به البديهة، فمفهوما الفرس واللافرس المأخوذان على هذا الوجه متباعدان في أنفسهما غاية التباعد، ومتدافعان الصدق على ذات واحدة فهما متقابلان بهذا الاعتبار.
The fourth observation – the eleventh section: The two opposites are two things that do not coexist. Since no negation or affirmation can be conceived except upon a relation; because if you consider a single concept, and do not consider with it a relation to another concept, nor a relation of another concept to it, you would have no perception of occurrence or non-occurrence attached to that single concept, as the intuition attests. So the two concepts 'horse' and 'non-horse' taken in this manner are extremely distant in themselves, and repel the truth of being attributed to one essence; thus they are opposites in this consideration.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of mutaqabilan (two opposites) and explains why they cannot coexist. It begins by stating that opposition fundamentally involves a relation (nisba). The author argues that affirmation and negation are only meaningful when applied to a relation between two concepts. If you take a single concept without relating it to another, you cannot perceive its occurrence or non-occurrence. For example, the concepts 'horse' and 'non-horse' when considered in isolation (without being attributed to a subject) are simply different in meaning but not yet opposites in the technical sense. However, when they are considered with respect to a single essence (dhat), they are mutually exclusive — the same thing cannot be both horse and non-horse. Thus, they become opposites in this relational consideration. The point is that opposition requires a common subject of predication.

Correction: The passage shows that true opposition requires a relation to a common subject; two concepts that are merely different cannot be called opposites in the technical sense.

Opposition and the Locus: From Substances to Accidents
Objection and answer: opposition requires a locus; answer shifts to accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector claims that 'horse' and 'non-horse' are not opposites and how the author responds by shifting to accidental concepts with a unique opposition outside the four standard categories.

Core Idea
Opposition can occur in accidents beyond the usual four categories.

This passage presents a sharp objection: if opposition requires a locus (a subject of inherence), then concepts like 'horse' and 'non-horse' cannot be opposites because they are not accidental qualities that inhere in a substrate. The author's response is to shift the discussion to accidental concepts like whiteness and non-whiteness, which do inhere in a locus and exhibit an opposition that falls outside the familiar fourfold division. Read on to see how this objection and answer refine our understanding of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of the argument. Then read the translation. Notice that the objection is signaled by 'فإن قلت' and the answer by 'قلت'.
The structure is standard kalam: objection followed by rebuttal.
2
Understand the objector's premise
The objector assumes that opposition requires a locus (mahall) or subject in which the opposites inhere. Since 'horse' and 'non-horse' are not accidental qualities that inhere in a substrate, they cannot be considered opposites.
This premise is rooted in the Aristotelian tradition where opposites are typically accidents inhering in a substance.
3
Grasp the author's shift to accidental concepts
The author does not directly refute the premise but shifts the example to accidental concepts—whiteness and non-whiteness—which do inhere in a locus. Between these, there is an opposition that is not one of the standard four divisions (tadadd, tadhayuf, milkat, etc.). This shows that opposition is broader than the objector assumed.
The author refers to an earlier discussion where this fifth type of opposition was indicated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : قد المعتبر في المتقابلين هو المحل، أو الموضوع وليس لمفهومي الفرس واللافرس حلول في محل، فلا تقابل بينهما . قلت : ينقل الكلام إلى مفهومي البياض واللابياض المأخوذين على الوجه الأخير فبينهما تقابل خارج عن الأقسام الأربعة، كما أشرنا إليه .
If you say: The consideration in the two opposites is the locus (mahall) or the subject, and the concepts of horse and non-horse have no subsistent in a locus, so there is no opposition between them. I say: The discussion shifts to the concepts of whiteness and non-whiteness taken in the latter manner, for between them is an opposition external to the four divisions, as we have pointed out.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that opposition requires a locus (mahall) or subject in which the opposites inhere. Since concepts like horse and non-horse do not inhere in a substrate like accidents do, they cannot be considered opposites. The author responds by shifting the example to whiteness (bayad) and non-whiteness (la-bayad), which are accidental concepts that do inhere in a locus. Between these there is an opposition that falls outside the four standard categories of opposition (tadadd, tadhayuf, milkat, etc.), as indicated earlier.

Essential Opposition: Negation and Affirmation
Author concludes that essential opposition is only between negation and affirmation; others are by entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why essential opposition belongs exclusively to negation and affirmation, and how other opposites are only opposed by entailment.

Core Idea
Only negation and affirmation are true opposites; all other opposites are derivative.

In this concluding passage, the author clarifies a fundamental principle of logical opposition: true essential opposition is found only between a statement and its negation. Other pairs, like black and white, are opposites only because each one forces the absence of the other. This distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in theology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two types of opposition
Notice how the author distinguishes between essential opposition (negation/affirmation) and derivative opposition (all other pairs). Understand the reasoning: essential opposition is based on the essences themselves.
3
Examine the example of black and white
Consider why black and white are not essential opposites. The author says they are opposites only because each entails the negation of the other.
If it were not for that entailment, they would not be opposites.
4
Reflect on the strength of essential incompatibility
The author concludes that the incompatibility between negation and affirmation is stronger because it is essential. Consider why this matters for logical argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة المقصد الحادي عشر ) التقابل بالذات إنما هو بين السلب والإيجاب ) ؛ لأن امتناع الاجتماع بينهما إنما هو بالنظر إلى ذاتيهما ، ( وغيرهما من الأقسام إنما يثبت فيها التقابل ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما مستلزم لسلب الآخر، ولولاه ) أي لولا استلزام كل منهما لسلب الآخر (لم يتقابلا، فإن معنى التقابل ذلك)، أي استلزام كل منهما سلب الآخر. فلولا أن كل واحد من السواد والبياض يستلزم عدم الآخر لم يتقابلا أصلاً، فالتنافي بين السلب والإيجاب بالذات وفي سائر الأقسام بتوسطهما ولا شك أن التنافي في الذات أقوى
Conclusion of the eleventh section: Essential opposition is only between negation (salb) and affirmation (ijab); because the impossibility of their coexistence is considered with regard to their essences. As for other divisions, opposition is established in them only because each one of them entails the negation of the other. Were it not for that (i.e., without each entailing the negation of the other), they would not be opposites. For the meaning of opposition is that: each entails the negation of the other. For if each of blackness and whiteness did not entail the non-existence of the other, they would not be opposites at all. So the incompatibility between negation and affirmation is essential, while in other divisions it is mediated by them. And there is no doubt that essential incompatibility is stronger.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the concluding statement of the eleventh section. It asserts that true essential opposition (taqabul bi al-dhat) belongs solely to the pair of affirmation and negation (ijab wa salb). This is because their mutual exclusion stems from their own essences — a thing cannot simultaneously be both affirmed and negated of the same subject. In contrast, other types of opposition (like black and white) are only opposed because each entails the negation of the other by means of something else. The example given: blackness and whiteness would not be opposites if each did not logically imply the absence of the other. The author emphasizes that the conflict (tanafi) in the case of ijab and salb is direct and stronger than in other categories.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all opposites are equally essential
Correction: Only negation and affirmation are essential opposites; others like black and white are opposed only because each entails the negation of the other.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are always found together.
B) Because the impossibility of their coexistence is due to their own essences.
C) Because they are both simple concepts.
D) Because they are both relational.

Key Takeaway
True opposition is only between affirmation and negation; all other opposites depend on that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it look like to bring your affirmation into full consistency, without hidden negations?
?
The author says essential incompatibility is stronger. How does this principle challenge you to be more decisive in your faith? Where do you need to stop trying to hold onto both a truth and its opposite, and instead embrace the negation wholeheartedly?

The Two Senses of Affirmation and Negation in Opposition
Ibn Sina's distinction between simple and compound opposites via al-Shifa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina distinguishes between general and specific meanings of affirmation and negation, and why this distinction resolves a dispute about the number of categories of opposition.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation have two senses, changing how we classify opposites.

This passage tackles a subtle point in logic. Scholars dispute whether opposites can be reduced to four categories. Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' provides the key: the terms 'affirmation' and 'negation' can be used in a broad or narrow sense. Understanding this distinction resolves the apparent conflict. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Shifa'
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that Ibn Sina first defines two kinds of opposites: simple and compound.
2
Identify the key distinction
What distinguishes simple from compound opposites? Note the truth-value criterion.
Simple opposites like 'horseness' don't have truth values; compound ones like 'Zayd is a horse' do.
3
Relate to the four categories
Understand that when scholars limit opposition to four categories, they are using 'affirmation' and 'negation' in the general sense that includes both simple and compound.
If they used the specific sense, the limitation would be invalid.
4
Reflect on the resolution
See how citing Ibn Sina resolves the dispute by clarifying the terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجاب بأن الشيخ قال في الشفاء : إن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب إن لم يحتملا الصدق والكذب، فبسيط كالفرسية واللافرسية، وإلا فمركب كقولنا زيد فرس زيد ليس بفرس، فمن حصر التقابل في الأقسام الأربعة أراد بالإيجاب والسلب المعنى العام الذي ذكره الشيخ، وإن كان إطلاق الإيجاب على أحد قسمي العام على سبيل الشبه والمجاز. نعم من حصر التقابل في الأربعة وأراد بالإيجاب والسلب المعنى الخاص ورد عليه بطلان الحصر.
The answer is that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) said in al-Shifa': The two opposites by affirmation and negation, if they do not admit truth and falsehood, are simple, like 'horseness' and 'non-horseness'; otherwise they are compound, like our saying 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse.' So whoever confines opposition to the four categories intends by affirmation and negation the general meaning mentioned by the Shaykh, even if the application of affirmation to one of the two divisions of the general is by way of analogy (shabah) and metaphor. Indeed, whoever confines opposition to the four categories and intends by affirmation and negation the specific meaning, the invalidity of the confinement is objected against him.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage addresses a disputed point. The commentator cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' to clarify the scope of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb) in the classification of opposites. Ibn Sina distinguishes between simple opposition (like 'horseness' and 'non-horseness') which do not have truth value, and compound opposition (like 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse') which do. The point is that when scholars limit opposition to four categories, they are using 'ijab' and 'salb' in a broad sense that includes both simple and compound. If someone uses the terms in a narrow sense (only propositional), then their claim that only four categories exist would be invalidated.

Key Terms

إيجاب وسلب عام
إيجاب وسلب خاص

مركب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four categories of opposition are absolute and apply regardless of how affirmation and negation are defined.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that the four categories only hold when 'affirmation' and 'negation' are taken in a general sense that includes both simple and compound opposites; if taken in the specific sense, the classification is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what distinguishes simple from compound opposites?
A) Simple opposites have truth value, compound do not.
B) Simple opposites do not admit truth and falsehood, compound do.
C) Simple opposites are always affirmative.
D) Compound opposites are single concepts.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's distinction between general and specific meanings of affirmation/negation resolves the apparent conflict over the number of categories of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a recent disagreement you had. Can you trace the conflict to a difference in the way each of you understood a key term? How would applying Ibn Sina's distinction between general and specific meanings of affirmation/negation have helped clarify the issue and potentially resolve it? What concrete step can you take this week to ensure you define terms clearly before entering a discussion?
?
The passage distinguishes between simple opposites (concepts without truth value) and compound opposites (propositions that can be true or false). Do you ever treat a mere concept as if it were a verifiable statement, leading to unnecessary certainty or doubt? Identify one concept you hold that might be just a simple opposition—neither true nor false—and consider how this realization frees you to approach it with more humility and openness to correction.

Essential Opposition: Affirmation vs. Negation
This passage defines essential opposition and proves its primacy over other types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essential opposition (al-taqābul bi al-dhāt) exists only between affirmation and negation, and that all other types of opposition derive from this fundamental relation.

Core Idea
Essential opposition is direct and intrinsic; all other oppositions borrow their force from affirmation and negation.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary clarifies a fundamental principle of logic: not all oppositions are created equal. We often think of opposites like black and white, but the most basic opposition is the one between existence and non-existence, affirmation and negation. Sanusi explains why this distinction matters and how it underlies every other form of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the opening correction of a mistaken view.
2
Identify the mistaken view
Find the sentence about 'horse' and 'non-horse'. Understand why the author considers this a misunderstanding unless based on superficial resemblance.
3
Locate the definition of essential opposition
Find where the passage states that essential opposition is only between negation and affirmation. Note the reason given: impossibility of conjunction by their essences.
4
Follow the proof for other types
Read the explanation that other types of opposition (like contrariety) derive from each term entailing the negation of the other. Note the example of black and white.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the mutual exclusion between affirmation and negation is stronger than in other types—it is direct, not mediated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن زغم أن بين الفرس واللافرس تقابل الإيجاب والسلب مطلقاً فقدسها، إلا أن يبني ذلك على الشبه والنظر إلى الظاهر.
خاتمة المقصد الحادي عشر ] ( التقابل بالذات إنما هو بين السلب والإيجاب ) ؛ لأن امتناع الاجتماع بينهما إنما هو بالنظر إلى ذاتيهما ، ( وغيرهما من الأقسام إنما يثبت فيها التقابل ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما مستلزم لسلب الآخر، ولولاه ) أي لولا استلزام كل منهما لسلب الآخر (لم يتقابلا، فإن معنى التقابل ذلك)، أي استلزام كل منهما سلب الآخر. فلولا أن كل واحد من السواد والبياض يستلزم عدم الآخر لم يتقابلا أصلاً، فالتنافي بين السلب والإيجاب بالذات وفي سائر الأقسام بتوسطهما ولا شك أن التنافي في الذات أقوى،
Whoever supposes that between 'horse' and 'non-horse' there is the opposition of affirmation and negation absolutely is mistaken, unless he bases that on resemblance and looking at the outward appearance. Conclusion of the eleventh goal: (Essential opposition is only between negation and affirmation); because the impossibility of conjunction between them is only by consideration of their essences. (And other types of opposition are only established in them because each of them entails the negation of the other, and were it not for that) i.e., were it not for each entailing the negation of the other (they would not be opposites, for the meaning of opposition is that), i.e., each entailing the negation of the other. Thus, were it not that each of blackness and whiteness entails the non-existence of the other, they would not be opposites at all. So the mutual exclusion between negation and affirmation is essential, and in other types it is by mediation of them, and there is no doubt that the essential exclusion is stronger.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by addressing a mistaken view: someone might think that the pair 'horse' and 'non-horse' exemplifies opposition of affirmation and negation absolutely. The commentator dismisses this as erroneous unless it is based on superficial resemblance. Then the core definition is stated: essential opposition (al-taqābul bi al-dhāt) exists only between negation and affirmation. The proof is that the impossibility of their coexistence is due to their essences themselves, not any external factor. Other types of opposition (like contrariety, correlation, etc.) derive their opposition from the fact that each term entails the negation of the other; without that entailment there would be no opposition. The example of blackness and whiteness illustrates this: they are opposites only because each entails the absence of the other, and this entailment is mediated by the underlying affirmation-negation relation. Therefore, the mutual exclusion between negation and affirmation is direct and essential, while in other types it is indirect, making the essential type stronger.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that essential opposition exists between any pair of concepts, such as black and white or horse and non-horse.
Correction: The passage insists essential opposition is only between affirmation and negation; all other oppositions are derivative and mediated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the opposition between affirmation and negation stronger than other types?
A) Because it involves two terms.
B) Because it is based on the essences themselves without mediation.
C) Because it is the most common type.
D) Because it is only found in simple terms.

Key Takeaway
Essential opposition is the strongest form because it arises directly from the natures of affirmation and negation themselves, without any mediation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says essential opposition is stronger. In defending truth against falsehood, do you rely on direct, essential arguments (like affirming God's oneness) or on secondary, derivative arguments? Which deserves more of your focus?
?
Is there a falsehood you tolerate in your life because you mistakenly think it is only 'accidentally' opposed to truth, not essentially? What would it mean to treat it as an essential opposition?
