General and Specific Meanings of Affirmation and Negation
Explanation of two senses of affirmation/negation and defense of commentator's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms 'affirmation' and 'negation' can be used in a general (apparent) sense and a specific (real) sense, and why this distinction resolves an apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's al-Shifa.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation have a real meaning and an apparent meaning; mixing them causes confusion.

In logic, the terms 'affirmation' and 'negation' are usually understood as properties of propositions. But as this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains, these terms can also be applied more broadly to simple terms like 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' based on a resemblance to the real meaning. This distinction helps refute an objection that Ibn Sina's generalizations contradict the commentator's definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get an overview of the argument.
2
Identify the two considerations
The passage speaks of a 'first consideration' and a 'second consideration'. Note how affirmation and negation are defined in each.
The second consideration is based on resemblance to the first.
3
Understand the example
The example of 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' shows how simple terms can be considered opposites by affirmation and negation under the second consideration.
4
Grasp the objection and response
An objection from al-Shifa is raised and refuted. Focus on why the commentator's position remains valid despite the generalization.
The key is distinguishing real vs. apparent meaning.
5
Reflect on the importance
Think about how this nuance prevents misunderstandings in later logical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا أن يبنى على الشبه إلخ) أي شبه الاعتبار الثاني بالاعتبار الأول في كون المفهومين في كل منهما في غاية التباعد فيراد بالإيجاب وجود أي معنى كان سواء كان وجوده في نفسه أو وجوده لغيره بالسلب لا وجود أي معنى كان سواء كان لا وجوده لغيره في نفسه، أو وجوده على ما وقع في الشفاء. فحينئذ يدخل نحو البياض واللابياض بالاعتبار الثاني في المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب. وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن ما قيل من أن مافي الشفاء من تعميم الإيجاب والسلب يدفع ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره ليس بشيء، لأن خلاصته أن تقابل الإيجاب والسلب بحسب الحقيقة لا يوجد في المفردين وبحسب الظاهر يوجد فيهما؛ إذ نحو البياض عنه، إذا أريد بالإيجاب والسلب ما هو في الحقيقة، وهو واللابياض خارج ينافي التعميم المستفاد من الشفاء.
His saying: 'unless he bases that on resemblance etc.' i.e., resemblance of the second consideration to the first consideration in that the two concepts in each are at the utmost distance; so by 'affirmation' is meant the existence of any meaning whatsoever, whether its existence is in itself or its existence for something else by negation, not the existence of any meaning whatsoever, whether its non-existence is for something else in itself or its existence, as occurred in al-Shifa. Then in that case, terms like whiteness and non-whiteness enter, by the second consideration, into the opposites by affirmation and negation. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that what is said – that the generalization of affirmation and negation in al-Shifa refutes what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned – is nothing, because the gist is that the opposition of affirmation and negation in reality does not exist in simple terms, but apparently it does exist in them; for example, whiteness and non-whiteness, if by affirmation and negation is meant what is in reality, they fall outside, contradicting the generalization obtained from al-Shifa.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and refutation. It clarifies a comment on the earlier statement about 'resemblance and outward appearance'. The commentator explains that the resemblance is between a 'second consideration' (al-iʿtibār al-thānī) and a 'first consideration'. In the first consideration, affirmation and negation are taken in their real sense, which requires the terms to be complex (propositions). In the second consideration, by resemblance to the first, affirmation is taken more broadly to mean existence of any meaning, whether self-subsistent or not, and negation correspondingly. This broader sense allows simple terms like 'whiteness' and 'non-whiteness' to be considered opposites of affirmation and negation. The passage then addresses an objection: someone claimed that the generalization of affirmation and negation in Ibn Sina's al-Shifa refutes the commentator's position. The response is that this generalization refers to the apparent meaning, not the real meaning; in reality, simple terms are not included, so there is no contradiction. The commentator's point stands.

Correction: The distinction between real and apparent meanings shows that simple terms can be considered opposites under the second consideration based on resemblance, not in reality.

C) In the sense of complex propositions only.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between the real and apparent senses of affirmation and negation resolves the apparent conflict between the commentator's definition and Ibn Sina's generalization.

Essential Opposition and the Role of Mediation
Definition of essential opposition and handling of unity/multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how 'essential opposition' is defined as the absence of mediation in affirmation, existence, and attribution, and why unity and multiplicity are not essential opposites.

Core Idea
Essential opposition means no mediation between opposites.

This passage begins by clarifying a key term: essential opposition. The commentator explains that it refers to opposition without any intermediary in existence or attribution. This is important because it resolves a potential contradiction regarding unity and multiplicity, which are opposites only by mediation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Notice the repeated phrase 'قوله' indicating commentary.
2
Identify the definition
Find where essential opposition is defined and what it excludes. The passage says it is 'the absence of mediation in affirmation, existence, and attribution'.
3
Understand the mediation issue
See how the passage reconciles the earlier claim that unity and multiplicity are not opposites essentially. The answer is that they are opposites by mediation, so they are not essential opposites.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( التقابل بالذات) بمعنى انتفاء الواسطة في الإثبات والثبوت والعروض، كما يدل عليه تعليل الشارح قدس سره .
قوله : ( إنما يثبت فيها التقابل لأن إلخ) ففي جميعها يتحقق الواسطة في الثبوت، فهذا الحكم لا ينافي ما تقدم من أن الوحدة والكثرة لا تقابل بينهما بالذات، بل بواسطة المكيالية والمكيلية؛ لأن بالذات هناك في مقابلة بالعرض.
قوله : ( بتوسطهما ) أي هما واسطة في الثبوت .
يجاب بأن الشيخ قال في الشفاء : إن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب إن لم يحتملا الصدق والكذب، فبسيط كالفرسية واللافرسية، وإلا فمركب كقولنا زيد ،فرس زيد ليس بفرس، فمن حصر التقابل في الأقسام الأربعة أراد بالإيجاب والسلب المعنى العام الذي ذكره الشيخ، وإن كان إطلاق الإيجاب على أحد قسمي العام على سبيل الشبه والمجاز. نعم من حصر التقابل في الأربعة وأراد بالإيجاب والسلب المعنى الخاص ورد عليه بطلان الحصر.
قوله: (وغيرهما من الأقسام إلخ أما في تقابل التضاد والتضايف فظاهر، وأما في تقابل
المرصد الرابع - خاتمة المقصد الحادي عشر
وأيضاً ( فالخير فيه أنه ليس بشر، وهو) أي نفي الشر عن الخير أمر (عارض) له، خارج عن ماهية الخيرية وفيه : أنه خير وهو ذاتي ( للخير ليس بخارج عن ماهيته ( وكونه شراً ينفي) عنه (كونه عارضاً له وهو نفي الشرية، وكونه ليس خيراً ينفي ) عنه ( الذاتي) الذي هو الخيرية ( والنافي للذاتي أقوى) في النفي وامتناع الاجتماع من النافي للعرضي ( فهو( أي تقابل السلب والإيجاب ( أقوى التقابلات وقيل: بل الأقوى هو التضاد ؛ إذ فيهما ) أي في المتضادين (مع السلب ) الضمني ( أمر آخر زائد، وهو غاية الخلاف المعتبرة في التضاد الحقيقي .
قوله : ( إن التنافي في الذاتي أقوى ؛ لكونه مقتضى الذات كوجود الواجب . قوله : غاية الخلاف المعتبرة إلخ يعني أن غاية الخلاف وإن تحقق في المتقابلين في الإيجاب والسلب، فهي ليست بمعتبرة فيهما، بخلاف المتضادين، فيكون تنافيهما أشد .
•
العدم والملكة، فلأن مفهوم العمى سلب البصر مقيداً بكون المحل قابلاً له، وهذا السلب المقيد مستلزم لسلب البصر مطلقاً . قوله : ( والنافي للذاتي أقوى اعترض عليه بأن العرضي إذا كان لازماً كان رافعه رافعاً للملزوم أيضاً، وإن لم يكن لازماً لم يكن رافعه منافياً لمعروضه لا يقال : إن الرافع بلا واسطة يكون أقوى من الرافع بواسطة الافتقار في التأثير إلى غيره ؛ لأن نقول : النار القوية قد تسخن بالواسطة تسخيناً أقوى من تسخين النار الضعيفة المؤثرة بلا واسطة، فلم لا يكون الأمر هاهنا كذلك. والحق : أن رفع الذاتي إذا كان رفعاً للماهية نفسها، كما ادعاه الشارح فيما سبق، يكون رافع الذاتي أقوى في النفي، والمعاندة من الرافع للعرضي؛ لأن رفعه مستلزم لرفع الماهية لا نفسه . قوله : (وقيل: بل الأقوى هو التضاد قائله صاحب التجريد على ما في بعض نسخه ورد بأنه لا يتصور اختلاف فوق التنافي الذاتي، بأن يكون أحدهما صريح سلب الآخر، وقيل : معنى كلامه أن أشد الأنواع في التشكيك هو التضاد ؛ لأن قبول القوة والضعف في أصنافه الحركة والسكون، والحرارة والبرودة، والسواد والبياض، وغير ذلك في غاية الظهور، بخلاف البواقي .
His saying: 'Essential opposition' meaning the absence of mediation in affirmation, existence, and attribution, as indicated by the commentator's reasoning, may his secret be sanctified. His saying: 'Opposition is established in them only because etc.' So in all of them mediation in existence occurs, so this ruling does not contradict what preceded that unity and multiplicity are not opposites essentially, but by mediation of being measure and measured; because 'essential' there is in contrast to accidental. His saying: 'by mediation of them' i.e., they are a mediation in existence. It is answered that the shaykh said in al-Shifa: that the opposites by affirmation and negation, if they do not admit truth and falsehood, are simple like 'horseness and non-horseness', otherwise they are complex like our saying 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse'. So whoever restricts opposition to the four types intends by affirmation and negation the general meaning mentioned by the shaykh, even if applying affirmation to one of the two parts of the general is by way of resemblance and metaphor. Yes, whoever restricts opposition to four and intends by affirmation and negation the specific meaning is refuted by the falsity of the restriction. His saying: 'and other types etc.' as for the opposition of contrariety and correlation it is apparent, and as for the opposition of... The fourth observation: conclusion of the eleventh goal. And also: (The good, in it is that it is not evil, and it) i.e., negating evil from the good is an accidental matter, external to the essence of goodness; and in it: it is good, and it is essential (to the good not external to its essence, and its being evil negates) from it (its being accidental to it, and its being not good negates) from it (the essential) which is goodness. (And the negator of the essential is stronger) in negation and impossibility of conjunction than the negator of the accidental. (So it) i.e., the opposition of negation and affirmation (is the strongest of oppositions. And it was said: rather the strongest is contrariety; because in them) i.e., in the two contraries (with the implicit negation there is an extra matter, which is the extreme difference considered in true contrariety. His saying: 'The mutual exclusion in the essential is stronger because it is a requirement of the essence, like the existence of the Necessary.' His saying: 'the extreme difference considered etc.' means that although extreme difference occurs in the two opposites in affirmation and negation, it is not considered in them, unlike the two contraries, so their mutual exclusion is more severe. Privation and possession: for the concept of blindness is the negation of sight restricted by the subject being capable of it, and this restricted negation entails the negation of sight absolutely. His saying: 'And the negator of the essential is stronger' – it is objected that if the accidental is necessary, its remover removes the essential as well, and if it is not necessary, its remover is not incompatible with its subject. It is not said that the remover without mediation is stronger than the remover by mediation due to need of another in effect, because we say: a strong fire may heat by mediation more strongly than the heating of a weak fire acting without mediation. So why is it not the case here? And the truth is: that removing the essential, if it is removal of the essence itself, as the commentator claimed previously, then the remover of the essential is stronger in negation and opposition than the remover of the accidental, because its removal entails the removal of the essence, not itself. His saying: 'And it was said: rather the strongest is contrariety' – its proponent is the author of al-Tajrid according to some copies; and it is refuted by the fact that no difference beyond essential mutual exclusion can be conceived, as when one of them is the explicit negation of the other. And it was said: the meaning of his speech is that the most severe type in terms of gradation is contrariety, because the acceptance of intensity and weakness in its kinds – motion and stillness, heat and cold, blackness and whiteness, and others – is most apparent, unlike the rest.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the strength of different types of opposition. It begins by clarifying that 'essential opposition' means absence of mediation in affirmation and existence. The commentator then explains that in other types, mediation exists, so the earlier claim about unity and multiplicity not being essential opposites is not contradicted. A response is given regarding the shaykh's (Ibn Sina's) view in al-Shifa: simple terms like 'horseness and non-horseness' fall under the general meaning of affirmation and negation. Then the passage enters the main comparison: it first argues that the opposition of affirmation and negation is the strongest because it negates the essence itself, as illustrated by the example of good and evil. An objection is raised that if the accidental is necessary, removing it removes the essential; the counterargument uses the analogy of fire to show that mediation can sometimes produce a stronger effect. The truth is that removing the essential removes the essence itself, making it stronger. Then a different opinion is cited from the author of al-Tajrid that contrariety is stronger, but it is refuted because no difference can exceed essential mutual exclusion. Another interpretation says that contrariety has gradation (shidda wa da'f), which makes it more severe in a sense, but the passage maintains the primacy of affirmation-negation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for opposition to be called 'essential'?
A) It must involve a middle term.
B) It must have no mediation in affirmation, existence, and attribution.
C) It must be between unity and multiplicity.
D) It must be accidental.

Key Takeaway
Essential opposition requires no mediation in existence, while other oppositions involve mediation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily struggle against sin, do you allow a 'mediation'—like rationalizing or delaying repentance—that makes you treat obedience and disobedience as something less than essential opposites? What would it mean to remove that mediation and confront the opposition directly?
?
The lesson says essential opposition means no mediation in existence. Think of a relationship or commitment in your life (e.g., to a person or to Allah) where you have placed a 'middle term' of excuses or half-measures. Can you identify that mediation and resolve to remove it?
?
Unity and multiplicity are opposed only by mediation—but in your own heart, is your tawḥīd free from any 'measure' or 'standard' that you have set up between you and Allah? What would it take for your worship to be essentially directed to Him alone, without any intermediary or parallel aim?

Why Affirmation-Negation is the Strongest Opposition
Argument for affirmation-negation as strongest opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the opposition of affirmation and negation is the strongest because it negates the essence itself, illustrated by the example of good and evil.

Core Idea
Affirmation-negation negates the essence, making it the strongest opposition.

Having clarified essential opposition, the passage now moves to the main comparison. It argues that the opposition of affirmation and negation is the strongest because it directly negates the essential attribute. The example of good and evil shows that negating the essential is more fundamental than negating an accidental characteristic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the shaykh's view
Understand the distinction between simple and complex affirmation-negation. Simple terms like 'horseness and non-horseness' fall under the general meaning.
This sets the stage for the main argument.
2
Study the good/evil example
Follow the reasoning: good is essentially good, so negating it (not good) negates the essence. Negating evil (not evil) is accidental. The negation of the essential is stronger.
3
Compare with contrariety
Notice that contrariety involves an extra condition (extreme difference) but that does not make it stronger because it does not negate the essence directly.
4
Formulate the conclusion
Understand why al-salab wa al-ijab is considered the strongest: because it negates the essence, which is more fundamental than negating accidents.

قوله : ( إنما يثبت فيها التقابل لأن إلخ) ففي جميعها يتحقق الواسطة في الثبوت، فهذا الحكم لا ينافي ما تقدم من أن الوحدة والكثرة لا تقابل بينهما بالذات، بل بواسطة المكيالية والمكيلية؛ لأن بالذات هناك في مقابلة بالعرض.

•
العدم والملكة، فلأن مفهوم العمى سلب البصر مقيداً بكون المحل قابلاً له، وهذا السلب المقيد مستلزم لسلب البصر مطلقاً . قوله : ( والنافي للذاتي أقوى اعترض عليه بأن العرضي إذا كان لازماً كان رافعه رافعاً للملزوم أيضاً، وإن لم يكن لازماً لم يكن رافعه منافياً لمعروضه لا يقال : إن الرافع بلا واسطة يكون أقوى من الرافع بواسطة الافتقار في التأثير إلى غيره ؛ لأن نقول : النار القوية قد تسخن بالواسطة تسخيناً أقوى من تسخين النار الضعيفة المؤثرة بلا واسطة، فلم لا يكون الأمر هاهنا كذلك. والحق : أن رفع الذاتي إذا كان رفعاً للماهية نفسها، كما ادعاه الشارح فيما سبق، يكون رافع الذاتي أقوى في النفي، والمعاندة من الرافع للعرضي؛ لأن رفعه مستلزم لرفع الماهية لا نفسه . قوله : (وقيل: بل الأقوى هو التضاد قائله صاحب التجريد على ما في بعض نسخه ورد بأنه لا يتصور اختلاف فوق التنافي الذاتي، بأن يكون أحدهما صريح سلب الآخر، وقيل : معنى كلامه أن أشد الأنواع في التشكيك هو التضاد ؛ لأن قبول القوة والضعف في أصنافه الحركة والسكون، والحرارة والبرودة، والسواد والبياض، وغير ذلك في غاية الظهور، بخلاف البواقي .
His saying: 'Essential opposition' meaning the absence of mediation in affirmation, existence, and attribution, as indicated by the commentator's reasoning, may his secret be sanctified. His saying: 'Opposition is established in them only because etc.' So in all of them mediation in existence occurs, so this ruling does not contradict what preceded that unity and multiplicity are not opposites essentially, but by mediation of being measure and measured; because 'essential' there is in contrast to accidental. His saying: 'by mediation of them' i.e., they are a mediation in existence. It is answered that the shaykh said in al-Shifa: that the opposites by affirmation and negation, if they do not admit truth and falsehood, are simple like 'horseness and non-horseness', otherwise they are complex like our saying 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse'. So whoever restricts opposition to the four types intends by affirmation and negation the general meaning mentioned by the shaykh, even if applying affirmation to one of the two parts of the general is by way of resemblance and metaphor. Yes, whoever restricts opposition to four and intends by affirmation and negation the specific meaning is refuted by the falsity of the restriction. His saying: 'and other types etc.' as for the opposition of contrariety and correlation it is apparent, and as for the opposition of... The fourth observation: conclusion of the eleventh goal. And also: (The good, in it is that it is not evil, and it) i.e., negating evil from the good is an accidental matter, external to the essence of goodness; and in it: it is good, and it is essential (to the good not external to its essence, and its being evil negates) from it (its being accidental to it, and its being not good negates) from it (the essential) which is goodness. (And the negator of the essential is stronger) in negation and impossibility of conjunction than the negator of the accidental. (So it) i.e., the opposition of negation and affirmation (is the strongest of oppositions. And it was said: rather the strongest is contrariety; because in them) i.e., in the two contraries (with the implicit negation there is an extra matter, which is the extreme difference considered in true contrariety. His saying: 'The mutual exclusion in the essential is stronger because it is a requirement of the essence, like the existence of the Necessary.' His saying: 'the extreme difference considered etc.' means that although extreme difference occurs in the two opposites in affirmation and negation, it is not considered in them, unlike the two contraries, so their mutual exclusion is more severe. Privation and possession: for the concept of blindness is the negation of sight restricted by the subject being capable of it, and this restricted negation entails the negation of sight absolutely. His saying: 'And the negator of the essential is stronger' – it is objected that if the accidental is necessary, its remover removes the essential as well, and if it is not necessary, its remover is not incompatible with its subject. It is not said that the remover without mediation is stronger than the remover by mediation due to need of another in effect, because we say: a strong fire may heat by mediation more strongly than the heating of a weak fire acting without mediation. So why is it not the case here? And the truth is: that removing the essential, if it is removal of the essence itself, as the commentator claimed previously, then the remover of the essential is stronger in negation and opposition than the remover of the accidental, because its removal entails the removal of the essence, not itself. His saying: 'And it was said: rather the strongest is contrariety' – its proponent is the author of al-Tajrid according to some copies; and it is refuted by the fact that no difference beyond essential mutual exclusion can be conceived, as when one of them is the explicit negation of the other. And it was said: the meaning of his speech is that the most severe type in terms of gradation is contrariety, because the acceptance of intensity and weakness in its kinds – motion and stillness, heat and cold, blackness and whiteness, and others – is most apparent, unlike the rest.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the strength of different types of opposition. It begins by clarifying that 'essential opposition' means absence of mediation in affirmation and existence. The commentator then explains that in other types, mediation exists, so the earlier claim about unity and multiplicity not being essential opposites is not contradicted. A response is given regarding the shaykh's (Ibn Sina's) view in al-Shifa: simple terms like 'horseness and non-horseness' fall under the general meaning of affirmation and negation. Then the passage enters the main comparison: it first argues that the opposition of affirmation and negation is the strongest because it negates the essence itself, as illustrated by the example of good and evil. An objection is raised that if the accidental is necessary, removing it removes the essential; the counterargument uses the analogy of fire to show that mediation can sometimes produce a stronger effect. The truth is that removing the essential removes the essence itself, making it stronger. Then a different opinion is cited from the author of al-Tajrid that contrariety is stronger, but it is refuted because no difference can exceed essential mutual exclusion. Another interpretation says that contrariety has gradation (shidda wa da'f), which makes it more severe in a sense, but the passage maintains the primacy of affirmation-negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Objections and the Claim for Contrariety
Objections and alternative view on strength of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the superiority of affirmation-negation and the alternative view that contrariety is stronger, along with their refutations.

Core Idea
Contrariety's extra condition does not make it stronger than essential negation.

After establishing that affirmation-negation is strongest, the passage addresses objections. Some argue that contrariety is stronger because it involves a further quality of extreme difference. The passage refutes this by showing that no difference can exceed the direct negation of essence. An analogy about fire is also discussed to illustrate the role of mediation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about accidental/essential
Understand the criticism: if an accidental is necessary, removing it removes the essential as well, so the essential remover might not be stronger. Also, there is a point about mediation.
2
Follow the fire analogy
Analyze how the analogy challenges the notion that mediation weakens opposition. A strong fire can heat by mediation more than a weak fire without mediation.
This shows that mediation does not necessarily weaken the effect.
3
Examine the claim for contrariety
See the argument from al-Tajrid that contrariety is stronger because it has extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf).
4
Understand the refutation
Learn why the claim is refuted: essential exclusion cannot be surpassed, and contrariety only adds gradation (tashkik).
The refutation relies on the concept that no difference can exceed one being the explicit negation of the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think contrariety is stronger because it involves an extra condition of extreme difference, making the mutual exclusion more severe.
Correction: The passage argues that essential negation is more fundamental, and extreme difference does not surpass the direct negation of essence. Contrariety only excels in gradation, not in absolute strength.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that contrariety is stronger ultimately refuted?
A) Contrariety does not involve extreme difference.
B) Essential mutual exclusion cannot be surpassed.
C) Contrariety has no gradation.
D) The author of al-Tajrid was mistaken about the types.

Key Takeaway
The strength of opposition is determined by negation of essence, not by additional conditions like extreme difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled for merely opposing a falsehood (contrariety) rather than uprooting its very essence from your heart? What one belief or habit will you commit to negating essentially this week?
?
The passage shows that a strong fire can heat indirectly more than a weak fire directly. Where in your life have you relied on your own direct strength when God's mediated means would be stronger? Will you trust His appointed means over your own?

Essential Negation and the Strength of Affirmation-Negation
Sanusi defines essential and accidental attributes and argues that negating an essential is stronger, thus affirmation-negation is the strongest opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposition of affirmation and negation is considered the strongest form of opposition, based on the distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Core Idea
Negating an essential attribute is stronger than negating an accidental attribute.

Sanusi begins by analyzing the nature of goodness and evil to illustrate a key principle: essential attributes are directly tied to the essence, so their negation is more fundamental. This leads him to declare that the opposition of affirmation and negation is the strongest type of opposition. Let's see how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the good/evil example and how Sanusi distinguishes essential from accidental.
2
Identify essential vs accidental
Note that goodness is essential to a good thing, while evilness is accidental. This distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that negating the essential is stronger, making affirmation-negation opposition the strongest. Ask yourself: why does negating the essential have more force?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - خاتمة المقصد الحادي عشر وأيضاً ( فالخير فيه أنه ليس بشر، وهو) أي نفي الشر عن الخير أمر (عارض) له، خارج عن ماهية الخيرية وفيه : أنه خير وهو ذاتي ( للخير ليس بخارج عن ماهيته ( وكونه شراً ينفي) عنه (كونه عارضاً له وهو نفي الشرية، وكونه ليس خيراً ينفي ) عنه ( الذاتي) الذي هو الخيرية ( والنافي للذاتي أقوى) في النفي وامتناع الاجتماع من النافي للعرضي ( فهو( أي تقابل السلب والإيجاب ( أقوى التقابلات وقيل: بل الأقوى هو التضاد ؛ إذ فيهما ) أي في المتضادين (مع السلب ) الضمني ( أمر آخر زائد، وهو غاية الخلاف المعتبرة في التضاد الحقيقي . قوله : ( إن التنافي في الذاتي أقوى ؛ لكونه مقتضى الذات كوجود الواجب . قوله : غاية الخلاف المعتبرة إلخ يعني أن غاية الخلاف وإن تحقق في المتقابلين في الإيجاب والسلب، فهي ليست بمعتبرة فيهما، بخلاف المتضادين، فيكون تنافيهما أشد . • العدم والملكة، فلأن مفهوم العمى سلب البصر مقيداً بكون المحل قابلاً له، وهذا السلب المقيد مستلزم لسلب البصر مطلقاً . قوله : ( والنافي للذاتي أقوى اعترض عليه بأن العرضي إذا كان لازماً كان رافعه رافعاً للملزوم أيضاً، وإن لم يكن لازماً لم يكن رافعه منافياً لمعروضه لا يقال : إن الرافع بلا واسطة يكون أقوى من الرافع بواسطة الافتقار في التأثير إلى غيره ؛ لأن نقول : النار القوية قد تسخن بالواسطة تسخيناً أقوى من تسخين النار الضعيفة المؤثرة بلا واسطة، فلم لا يكون الأمر هاهنا كذلك. والحق : أن رفع الذاتي إذا كان رفعاً للماهية نفسها، كما ادعاه الشارح فيما سبق، يكون رافع الذاتي أقوى في النفي، والمعاندة من الرافع للعرضي؛ لأن رفعه مستلزم لرفع الماهية لا نفسه . قوله : (وقيل: بل الأقوى هو التضاد قائله صاحب التجريد على ما في بعض نسخه ورد بأنه لا يتصور اختلاف فوق التنافي الذاتي، بأن يكون أحدهما صريح سلب الآخر، وقيل : معنى كلامه أن أشد الأنواع في التشكيك هو التضاد ؛ لأن قبول القوة والضعف في أصنافه الحركة والسكون، والحرارة والبرودة، والسواد والبياض، وغير ذلك في غاية الظهور، بخلاف البواقي .
The Fourth Observatory – Conclusion of the Eleventh Goal. And also: (Goodness in it is that it is not evil, and it) i.e., negating evil from good is a (accidental) attribute for it, external to the essence of goodness. And in it: that it is good, and it is essential (to the good, not external to its essence). And its being evil negates (from it) (its being an accidental attribute for it, and it is the negation of evilness), and its not being good negates (from it) (the essential) which is goodness. And the negator of the essential is stronger in negation and in preventing coexistence than the negator of the accidental. (So it), i.e., the opposition of negation and affirmation, (is the strongest of oppositions). And it was said: Rather, the strongest is contrariety; since in them, i.e., in the two contraries, (along with the implicit negation) there is an additional matter, which is the utmost disagreement considered in genuine contrariety. His statement: (Indeed, the opposition in the essential is stronger because it is a requirement of the essence, like the existence of the Necessary). His statement: The utmost disagreement considered etc. – meaning that although the utmost disagreement occurs in the two opposites in affirmation and negation, it is not considered in them, unlike the two contraries, so their opposition is more intense. • Privation and possession – for the concept of blindness is the negation of sight restricted by the substrate being capable of it, and this restricted negation entails the negation of sight absolutely. His statement: (And the negator of the essential is stronger) – an objection was raised against it: the accidental, if it is necessary, then its remover also removes the necessary concomitant, and if it is not necessary, its remover is not opposed to its subject. It should not be said: The remover without mediation is stronger than the remover with mediation due to needing another in effect; because we say: A strong fire may heat by mediation with a stronger heating than the heating of a weak fire acting without mediation, so why should the matter here not be the same? And the truth: that the removal of the essential, if it is a removal of the essence itself, as the commentator claimed previously, then the remover of the essential is stronger in negation and opposition than the remover of the accidental; because its removal entails the removal of the essence, not itself. His statement: (And it was said: Rather, the strongest is contrariety) – its proponent is the author of al-Tajrīd according to some of his copies – it was refuted by that no difference is conceivable above essential opposition, such that one of them is the explicit negation of the other. And it was said: The meaning of his statement is that the most intense of the types in gradation is contrariety; because the acceptance of strength and weakness in its species – motion and rest, heat and cold, blackness and whiteness, and other things – is extremely apparent, unlike the rest.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about which type of opposition (taqābul) is the strongest. It begins by distinguishing between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʿaraḍī) attributes using the example of goodness and evil. The author argues that negating an essential attribute (e.g., saying something is 'not good') is a stronger negation than negating an accidental attribute (e.g., saying it is 'not evil') because the essential directly belongs to the essence. Therefore, the opposition of affirmation and negation (salb wa ījāb) is declared the strongest form of opposition. Then a counterclaim is introduced: some say contrariety (tadādd) is stronger because contraries involve an additional element of maximal difference (ghāyat al-khilāf). The commentator explains that while maximal difference exists in affirmation-negation, it is not considered there as it is in contraries. Next, the passage touches on privation and possession (ʿadam wa mulka), using the example of blindness as a restricted negation. An objection is raised against the claim that the negator of the essential is stronger: if the accidental is a necessary concomitant, removing it removes the essential, so why is it weaker? The response uses the analogy of fire heating through mediation. The truth is that removing the essential removes the essence itself, making its negation more fundamental. Finally, the passage reports a view from the author of al-Tajrīd that contrariety is the strongest, but it is refuted: no opposition can exceed essential opposition where one term is the explicit negation of the other. An alternative interpretation is offered: contrariety is the most intense in terms of gradation (tashkīk), as its instances show clear degrees.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
سلب وإيجاب
تقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what makes the opposition of affirmation and negation the strongest?
A) It negates the accidental attribute.
B) It negates the essential attribute.
C) It involves both essential and accidental negation.
D) It is the only true opposition.

Key Takeaway
The opposition of affirmation and negation is strongest because it involves negating an essential attribute directly tied to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you negate false beliefs in your creed (like 'God is not unjust'), are you treating those negations as equally powerful? Or do some negations cut deeper because they touch the essence of who God is? How does knowing the strongest negation is the one that negates the essential attribute affect how you articulate your faith?
?
Think of an attribute you consider essential to your identity as a Muslim (e.g., 'I am a believer'). If someone negates that ('You are not a believer'), does it hit you harder than negating something accidental ('You are not a good speaker')? Why? How does this lesson challenge you to distinguish between essential and accidental aspects of your own faith?
?
In your daily life, you constantly affirm and negate things about God: 'He is All-Merciful' and 'He is not unjust.' Which negation carries more weight for your trust in Him? Are you neglecting the stronger negation that affirms His essential goodness? What commitment does this call you to make today?

Counterclaims: Contrariety and the Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the view that contrariety is strongest, and replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counterclaim that contrariety might be stronger, and how the author refutes it, as well as the objection regarding essential vs accidental negation.

Core Idea
Despite appearances, the removal of the essential is still the strongest form of negation.

After establishing that affirmation-negation is strongest, the author considers an alternative view: contrariety (tadādd) might be stronger because it involves maximal difference. He also addresses an objection about accidental attributes that are necessary concomitants. This lesson unpacks these nuanced discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterclaim
Read the passage from 'And it was said: Rather, the strongest is contrariety' up to the privation example. Understand why contrariety might be considered stronger.
2
Examine the objection
Read the objection: if an accidental attribute is a necessary concomitant, then removing it also removes the essential. Why would that not make the negation of the accidental equally strong?
3
Follow the response
Read the response with the fire analogy. Reflect on how mediation affects strength. Then note the ultimate truth: removal of the essential directly removes the essence.
4
Understand the final refutation
The view that contrariety is strongest is refuted: no opposition can exceed essential negation. The alternative interpretation about gradation is given.

The Simplicity of Substance Challenged
Criticizes the proof from substance by denying its simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from substance fails if substance is not simple, and how the philosophers' division of substance into five categories complicates the argument.

Core Idea
Substance is not simple; its multiple aspects break the proof.

In this passage, the commentator challenges a key assumption of the proof from substance: that substance is a simple, indivisible cause. He argues that substance has multiple dimensions — existence, quiddity, possibility, etc. — and is divided into five categories. This undermines the inference that all contingent beings must depend on a single substance. Let's unpack the objection and the attempted defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the commentator starts by saying the proof cannot be taken as binding because substance has five divisions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the non-simplicity
The core objection is that substance is not simple; it has existence, quiddity, possibility, genus, differentia, etc. Identify these aspects.
3
Analyze the counterargument
The opponent argues that despite having these aspects, substance is still one thing and each effect depends on it as a whole. Consider why this defense fails.
Pay attention to the refutation based on nobility of attributes.
4
List the five divisions
The passage ends by stating that substance is divided into five categories. Find them in the Persian note at the end.
These are: intellect, soul, body, matter, and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمكن أخذه إلزامياً لأن الجوهر عندهم خمسة أقسام، والقابل منها ابتداء، إذ بعد ثبوت هذا القول لا حاجة لنا إلى إثبات ذلك الجواز، فلا يرد أن هذا الاستدلال لا يتم إلا ببيان بساطة العلة التي هي الجوهرية مع أنها ليست بسيطة، فإن لها وجوداً وماهية وإمكاناً وجنساً وفصلاً، وغير ذلك. فإن قلت: هي بجميع ما فيها ولها شيء واحد مستند إليه، كل من الأمرين ولا معنى لاستناد الكثير إلى الواحد سوى هذا والحاصل: أن المناقشة إنما ترد إذا استند أحد الأمرين إليها باعتبار بعض جهاتها، والآخر باعتبار جهتها الأخرى، وهاهنا ليس كذلك قلت لا نسلم أنه ليس كذلك فإن الوجود أشرف من الإمكان، وقبول الأعراض لكون متبوعه أشرف من التحيز الذي يفيد الاحتياج إلى الحيز فجاز أن يستند الأشرف إلى الأشرف، والأخس إلى الأخس كما علم من قاعدتهم في بيان كيفية صدور الممكنات عن الواجب. قوله: ( لأن الجوهر عندهم خمسة أقسام أشياء خمسة كه زجوهر عبارت است عقل است ونفس وجسم وهيولي وصورة أست.
And it cannot be taken as binding because substance according to them has five divisions, and the one capable of it from the beginning, since after the establishment of this statement we have no need to prove that possibility. So it is not objected that this inference is not completed except by stating the simplicity of the cause which is substantiality, while it is not simple, for it has existence, quiddity, possibility, genus, differentia, and other things. For if you say: It is, with all that it contains and has, one thing, and each of the two matters is dependent on it, and there is no meaning to the dependence of many on one except this. And the sum: That the objection only occurs if one of the two matters depends on it by consideration of some of its aspects, and the other by consideration of its other aspect, and here it is not so. I say: We do not accept that it is not so, for existence is more noble than possibility, and accepting accidents because its subject is more noble than occupying space which implies need for space, so it is possible that the more noble depends on the more noble, and the lower on the lower, as is known from their principle in explaining how contingent things proceed from the Necessary. His statement: (Because substance according to them has five divisions: five things, that is, substance is intellect, soul, body, matter, and form.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage criticizes the proof from substance. It states that the proof cannot be taken as binding (إلزامياً) because substance is divided into five categories according to the philosophers: intellect, soul, body, matter, and form. If substance is not simple, the proof fails. The commentator then presents a possible counter: one might argue that even with all its aspects, substance is still one thing, and each effect depends on it as a whole; thus multiplicity arises from the single essence. But the commentator rejects this by noting that existence and accepting accidents are nobler than possibility and occupying space, so the nobler effects might depend on the nobler aspects, implying a distinction. Thus, the substance is not truly one from all aspects. The passage ends with a Persian note listing the five divisions of substance: intellect, soul, body, matter, and form.

بساطة
الوجود
الإمكان
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substance is a simple, indivisible entity.
Correction: According to the philosophers, substance has multiple aspects (existence, quiddity, etc.) and is divided into five categories, making it composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many divisions does substance (الجوهر) have?
A) Three.
B) Four.
C) Five.
D) Six.

Key Takeaway
The proof from substance fails because substance is not simple; it has multiple aspects and is divided into five categories, undermining the argument that all contingent beings depend on a single substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that assuming simplicity can lead to a flawed argument. In what area of your faith or life do you currently assume things are simpler than they really are? What would you need to investigate to see the full complexity, and will you do it this week?

The passage argues that substance has aspects with varying degrees of nobility. Which aspect of your own existence—your body, intellect, or soul—do you tend to neglect, and how can you restore the proper balance starting today?
?
If the philosophers' error was to ignore the internal complexity of substance, what 'substances' in your own belief system—core convictions or habits—have you never examined closely? Will you risk being unsettled by re-examining one of them?

Why Source-ness Does Not Cause an Infinite Chain
Author argues that source-ness is external and no chain occurs in conceptual matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of source-ness (المصدرية) does not require a separate cause and why infinite regress in conceptual matters is not problematic.

Core Idea
Relational concepts need no existence-causes; only real existents do.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: if multiple effects come from one cause, doesn't that create multiple 'source-nesses'? He explains that source-ness is a relational concept, not a real entity, so it doesn't need its own cause. Even if we imagine a chain, it would be mental, not real, so infinite regress is harmless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Get familiar with the key terms: المصدرية (source-ness), الأمور الإضافية (relational matters), الأمور الاعتبارية (conceptual matters).
Notice the author uses a conciliatory tone: 'نختار أن...' (we choose that...).
2
Focus on the core argument
Identify the author's main claim: المصدريتين خارجتان عن الواحد الحقيقي (the two source-nesses are external to the true one). Understand the reasoning: because source-ness is an الأمور الإضافية, it has no external existence, so it does not need a cause to bring it into existence. Hence, there is no other source-ness that would form a chain.
The phrase 'غير محتاجة إلى علة توجدها' is key: 'not in need of a cause to bring it into existence'.
3
Understand the concession about infinite regress
The author then concedes: even if we grant a chain of source-nesses, it would be a chain in conceptual matters (الأمور الاعتبارية). Such a chain is not impossible because it depends on mental consideration and stops when the intellect stops.
This shows the author's thoroughness in addressing potential objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : استناد آثار متعددة إلى مؤثر واحد
نختار أن المصدريتين خارجتان عن الواحد الحقيقي، إلا أن المصدرية لكونها من الأمور الإضافية التي لا وجودلها في الخارج غير محتاجة إلى علة توجدها ( فلا تكون الذات مصدراً لها؛ لأن المحتاج إلى الموجد ما له ،(وجود)، وحينئذ فلا يكون هناك مصدرية أخرى حتى تتسلسل المصدريات . وإن سلمنا ) تسلسها، فالتسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية غير ممتنع
قوله: (غير محتاجة إلى علة (توجدها وإن كانت محتاجة إلى علة للاتصاف بها، وهو البسيط الحقيقي، فكونها منتزعة من نفسه باعتبار استتباعها للأثر.
قوله : ( حتى تتسلسل المصدريات أي يحصل سلسلتها .
قوله : ( وإن سلمنا تسلسلها) يعني أن التسليم ليس راجعاً إلى كون الذات مصدراً لها، كما هو السابق إلى الفهم؛ لأنه لا يمكن حينئذ القول بأنه تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية، بل إلى ما يترتب عليه أعني التسلسل المشار إليه بقوله : حتى تتسلسل المصدريات أي إن سلمنا حصول سلسلة المصدريات بأن ينتزع العقل من كل مصدرية مصدرية أخرى نسبة بينها وبين البسيط الحقيقي، ، فهذا التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وهو غير ممتنع لأنه ينقطع بحسب انقطاع اعتبار العقل .
The Fifth Observatory - The Third Objective: The Attribution of Multiple Effects to One Efficient Cause.
We choose that the two source-nesses are outside the true one, except that source-ness, since it is among the relational matters that have no existence in the external, is not in need of a cause to bring it into existence (so the Essence is not a source for them; because that which needs an existent is that which has existence), and then there is no other source-ness so that the source-nesses would form a chain. And if we grant their chaining, then chaining in conceptual matters is not impossible.
His saying: (Not in need of a cause to bring it into existence) although it is in need of a cause for the attribution to it, which is the true simple, so its being derived from itself in consideration of its being subsequent to the effect.
His saying: (So that the source-nesses would form a chain) i.e., its chain would come to be.
His saying: (And if we grant its chaining) means that the granting does not refer to the Essence being a source for them, as is the initial understanding; because then it cannot be said that it is a chain in conceptual matters, but rather to what follows from it, namely the chain indicated by his saying: 'so that the source-nesses would form a chain', i.e., if we grant the occurrence of the chain of source-nesses by the intellect deriving from each source-ness another source-ness as a relation between it and the true simple, then this chain is in conceptual matters and it is not impossible because it is cut off according to the cessation of the intellect's consideration.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the author's position on the issue of multiple effects from a single cause. It is a definition and proof. The author states that the two 'source-nesses' (المصدرية) are external to the true one (the simple essence). However, he explains that source-ness is a relational matter (أمر إضافي) that has no external existence. Consequently, it does not require a cause to bring it into existence; only things with existence need causes. Therefore, the essence is not a source for them, and there is no other source-ness that would lead to an infinite chain of source-nesses. Even if we suppose such a chain, it would be in conceptual matters (أمور اعتبارية), and infinite regress in conceptual matters is not impossible because it is based on mental consideration. The commentary clarifies that the source-ness is a concept derived from the essence by virtue of its being followed by an effect, and it is not a real attribute. The chain of such mental considerations stops when the intellect stops considering.

Key Terms
المصدرية
الأمور الإضافية
التسلسل
الأمور الاعتبارية
البسيط الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every attribute or relation requires its own cause to exist.
Correction: Relational matters (الأمور الإضافية) have no external existence and thus do not need a cause to bring them into existence; they only need a cause for attribution (which is the essence itself).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the source-ness (المصدرية) not require a cause to bring it into existence?
A) Because it is a real attribute of the essence
B) Because it is among the relational matters that have no external existence
C) Because the essence itself is its cause
D) Because it is eternal

Key Takeaway
Source-ness is a relational concept, not a real entity, so it does not cause an infinite regress of causes; any mental chain is harmless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often attribute your achievements to your own effort. But according to this lesson, source-ness is a relational concept with no independent existence. How does this challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you today consciously attribute every effect to the true cause, Allah?
?
When you think about your sins, do you consider them as realities that need their own cause? Or do you see them as mere relational lacks? How might this distinction change the way you repent and turn back to Allah?
?
The author says conceptual chains are harmless and can be cut off by the intellect. Are there worries or fears you treat as real, unbreakable chains that are actually only mental constructs? What would it take for you to cut them off by considering their conceptual nature?

Two Meanings of Motion: Cutting and Intermediacy
Commentator distinguishes motion as cutting from motion as intermediacy and explains implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between motion as cutting and motion as intermediacy, and why intensity and speed apply only to the former.

Core Idea
Motion has two meanings: cutting (traversing distance) and intermediacy (instant-by-instant occupation); only cutting involves speed and intensity.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a crucial distinction in kalam physics. When discussing motion over a distance, the author specifies that the matn's statement refers to motion in the sense of 'cutting' (al-qaṭʿ), not 'intermediacy' (al-tawassuṭ). This distinction affects how we understand intensity, speed, and the relation of motion to time. Let's explore why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the two terms for motion.
The passage is dense; reading both versions helps.
2
Identify the two meanings of motion
Find where the commentator distinguishes motion as cutting (قطعت) from motion as intermediacy (توسط). Note how each is defined.
Cutting involves traversing a divisible distance; intermediacy is instant-by-instant.
3
Understand the implications for intensity and speed
The passage argues that intensity (شدة) and speed/slowness are only applicable to motion as cutting, not intermediacy. Ask yourself why: because speed requires covering a distance over time, which only cutting motion does.
This refutes the misconception that intensity applies to motion as intermediacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة إلخ ) المراد هو الحركة بمعنى القطع، وأما الحركة بمعنى التوسط فهي آنية، ولا يوصف الجسم بها باعتبار فعله إياها بالشدة ولا بعدم التناهي فيها، لأن الشدة في الحركة باعتبار سرعتها وعدم تناهيها في الشدة باعتبار أنها لا حركة أسرع منها، كما أشار إليه الشارح والسرعة والبطء باعتبار قطع المسافة، ولا قطع إلا بالحركة بمعنى القطع، وأيضاً عدم التناهي فيها باعتبار أن الزمان وصل بقبول الانفصالات الغير المتناهية إلى ما انطبق هذه الحركة عليها، كما عرفت . والزمان لا يصل إلى الآن أبداً عند الفلاسفة، ثم إن الحركة بمعنى القطع وإن كان أمراً وهمياً لكنهم يجرون عليها أحكام الموجود بناء على أنها من الأمر الموجود أعني الحركة بمعنى التوسط كما سيأتي، فلذلك اعتبر أثراً للقوة الجسمانية.
His statement: (Because every motion is only over a divisible distance, etc.) What is meant is motion in the sense of cutting (al-qaṭʿ), but motion in the sense of intermediacy (al-tawassuṭ) is instant-by-instant, and the body is not described by it in consideration of its being the doer of it with intensity, nor with infinity in it, because intensity in motion is by virtue of its speed, and its infinity in intensity is by virtue that there is no motion faster than it, as the commentator indicated. And speed and slowness are by virtue of cutting distance, and there is no cutting except by motion in the sense of cutting. Also, infinity in it is by virtue that time, by accepting infinite divisions, reaches what this motion is applied to, as you have known. And time never reaches an instant according to the philosophers. Then, although motion in the sense of cutting is an imaginary matter, they apply upon it the rulings of existence, based on the fact that it is from existing matters, namely motion in the sense of intermediacy, as will come. Therefore, it is considered an effect of physical force.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key distinction in the concept of motion. The commentator specifies that when the matn says 'every motion is over a divisible distance', it means motion in the sense of 'cutting' (al-qaṭʿ) — i.e., traversing a finite continuous distance — not motion in the sense of 'intermediacy' (al-tawassuṭ), which is the continual occupation of successive positions and is instant-by-instant (instantaneous, without duration). For motion as intermediacy, the body is not described as being intense or infinite in intensity, because intensity and speed relate to how quickly a distance is cut. Speed and slowness are only applicable when a distance is traversed, which requires motion in the cutting sense. Infinity in intensity is also defined by the impossibility of a faster motion; it does not imply actual indivisibility. The commentator notes that time, according to the philosophers, never reaches an instant because it is continuous; but the cutting sense of motion is an imaginary construct used to apply judgments of existence based on the actual motion-as-intermediacy. Therefore, the effect considered as a product of physical force is the cutting motion, even though it is mentally posited.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى القطع
الحركة بمعنى التوسط
الآن
الزمان
الشدة
السرعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intensity (شدة) applies to motion as intermediacy (توسط).
Correction: Intensity is defined by speed, which requires traversing a distance; motion as intermediacy is instantaneous, so it cannot have intensity.

Checkpoint
Which sense of motion is used when discussing speed and intensity of a physical force?
A) Motion as intermediacy (التوسط)
B) Motion as cutting (القطع)
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Only motion as cutting involves traversing a distance and thus speed and intensity; motion as intermediacy is instantaneous and described by continuous positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly move through life, but do you experience your actions as a continuous 'cutting' across time or as a series of isolated 'intermediacy' moments? How does this distinction affect the way you take responsibility for gradual change in your character?
?
The commentators call cutting motion 'imaginary' yet treat it as real for rulings. Consider your own intentions and spiritual goals—are they 'imaginary' constructs that you nevertheless treat as real? Will you dare to examine whether your daily actions flow from real moment-by-moment awareness or from a mere imagined trajectory?
?
Philosophers say time never reaches an instant. You live now—but is there a single 'instant' of your life that you fully own? If your every action is an indivisible now, what prevents you from making this present moment a decisive turning point toward Allah?

The First Objection: 'Total' Only for Finite
Objection and refutation about 'totality' only applying to finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that 'totality' only applies to finite collections is a verbal dispute that does not undermine the proof against infinite regress.

Core Idea
The concept of 'totality' is rationally coherent for both finite and infinite sets.

In this passage, an objector claims that the term 'totality' only applies to finite collections, aiming to block the use of 'totality' in the proof against infinite regress. The author refutes this as a mere verbal dispute, clarifying that the intended meaning of 'totality'—a collection from which nothing is excluded—is a rational consideration valid for both finite and infinite matters. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objector frames the limitation of the term 'total'.
The objection uses terms like 'جميع' and 'مجموع'.
2
Identify the refutation
The author immediately labels the objection as a 'verbal dispute' (نزاع لفظي). Then he clarifies the intended meaning of 'totality'.
3
Understand the clarification
The intended meaning is simply 'the collection from which nothing is excluded', which is rationally applicable to any collection, finite or infinite.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection might seem plausible, and how the clarification resolves it without undermining the proof.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول أن لفظ الجميع والمجموع والجملة إنما يطلق على المتناهي، وهذا نزاع لفظي؛ إذ المراد بالمجموع هاهنا هو تلك الأمور بحيث لا يخرج عنها واحد منها كما نبه عليه بقوله : ولا يخرج عنها شيء منها، وهذا اعتبار معقول في الأمور المتناهية وغير المتناهية .
The first [objection] is that the term 'all', 'totality', and 'whole' is only applied to the finite. But this is a verbal dispute; for what is meant by totality here is those things such that none of them is excluded, as he pointed out by his saying: 'and nothing of them is excluded,' and this is a rational consideration in both finite and infinite matters.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the proof that infinite regress is impossible. The objector claims that the concept of 'total' or 'totality' (al-jumla, al-majmu') is only applicable to finite collections, so one cannot speak of a totality in an infinite chain. The answer immediately refutes this as a verbal dispute (niza' lafzi). The intended meaning of 'totality' here is simply the collection of all individuals such that none is left out – a notion that is rationally coherent (i'tibar ma'qul) for both finite and infinite collections. Therefore, the objection does not undermine the proof.

Key Terms

مجموع
جملة
متناهي
نزاع لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'totality' is meaningless without a finite sum, so an infinite set cannot be considered a 'totality'.
Correction: The passage shows that a rational concept of completeness (nothing excluded) applies to infinite sets as well, so the term is not limited to the finite.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection that 'totality' only applies to finite things?
A) He agrees and redefines the argument accordingly.
B) He says it is a verbal dispute, and the intended meaning is rationally applicable to both finite and infinite.
C) He denies that infinite chains exist at all.
D) He argues that 'totality' is a metaphorical term.

Key Takeaway
The objection that 'totality' only applies to finite collections is a verbal dispute; the relevant meaning (nothing excluded) is rationally valid for all collections, whether finite or infinite.

?
The rational consideration of 'none excluded' applies to both finite and infinite. Does your understanding of Allah’s infinite nature affect how you think about completeness—for example, in your devotion, repentance, or trust? Are there areas where you treat God’s infinite attributes as if they were finite?

Burhan al-Tatbiq: The Core Proof Against Infinite Regress
Jurjani formulates the proof of alignment against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Burhan al-Tatbiq and how it demonstrates the impossibility of an actual infinite regress of causes.

Core Idea
Aligning two infinite series forces a contradiction, proving finiteness.

Al-Jurjani presents the most important argument in kalam against infinite regress: the proof of alignment. By assuming two parallel infinite series—one deficient, one excessive—and aligning them, a contradiction emerges: the deficient series must cut off, becoming finite, which contradicts the assumption of infinity. This lesson unpacks the proof's mechanics and its foundational role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview of the proof's flow.
Notice the key terms: naqisa (deficient) and za'ida (excessive).
2
Identify the two series
Understand that the proof hypothesizes two series: one deficient (missing a part) and one excessive (having an extra part). Both are assumed infinite.
3
Trace the alignment logic
Follow the reasoning: either the deficient series matches the excessive exactly (which makes the excess meaningless) or a part of the excessive has no counterpart, causing the deficient to cut off and become finite.
The contradiction arises because the deficient was assumed infinite but now is finite.
4
Understand the conclusion
If the deficient is finite and the excessive exceeds it by a finite amount, the excessive is also finite. This contradicts the initial assumption of both being infinite.
This contradiction shows that actual infinite regress cannot exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الناقصة المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة (كانت الناقصة كالزائدة ) ، أي مساوية لها في عدة الآحاد ، ( وهذا خلف . وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن بإزاء كل واحد من الزائدة واحد من الناقصة، ( وجد في الزائدة جزء لا يوجد بإزائه في الناقصة شيء، وعنده ) أي عند الجزء الذي لا يوجد بإزائه شيء من ) تنقطع الناقصة ) بالضرورة فتكون الناقصة (متناهية)، لانقطاعها . ( والزائدة لا تزيد عليها إلا بمتناه كما صورناه ( والزائد على المتناهي بمتناه متناه) بلا شبهة. ( فيلزم انقطاعهما وتناهيهما في الجهة التي فرضناهما غير متناهيتين وغير منقطعتين فيها، هذا خلف. وهذا الدليل هو المسمى ببرهان التطبيق، وهو ( العمدة ) في إبطال التسلسل لجريانه في الأمور المتعاقبة في الوجود، كالحركات الفلكية، وفي الأمور المجتمعة، سواء كان بينها ترتب طبيعي، كالعلل والمعلولات، أو وضعي كالأبعاد أو لا يكون هناك ترتب أصلاً، كالنفوس الناطقة المفارقة. وليس في قوله : (أي مساوية لها إلخ) بمعنى عدم المفاوتة، لأنه يوجد في كل واحدة منها ما يوجد الأخرى، فلا يكون الجزء جزءاً ولا الكل كلاً ويكون وجود الزيادة كالعدم وحينئذ سقط ما قيل لا نسلم لزوم التساوي إن أريد به نوافي الجملتين بحد واحد لأن الوجدان المذكور كما يكون لأجل التساوي يكون لأجل اللاتناهي أيضاً، وإن أريد به عدم المفاوتة فلا نسلم استحالته . قوله : ( فتكون الناقصة متناهية والمفروض عدم تناهيها هذا خلف فقوله والزائدة لا تزيد إلخ زيادة بيان يتم المدعى بدونها . قوله : ( والزائد على المتناهي) أي بمراتب متناهية . قوله : ( لجريانه إلخ) فعمديته باعتبار عموم التسلسل في جانب العلل . نفعه مع مساواته لما عداها في إفادة بطلان قوله : ( كانت الناقصة كالزائدة ) أي مساوية لها لأن الزيادة غير معقولة فكأنها غير محتملة على أن انقطاع الزائدة يستلزم التناهي وفيه المطلوب وهاهنا بحث وهو أنه إن أريد بكون الناقصة كالزائدة التساوي بمعنى توافي حد الجملتين فليس بلازم إذ لا حد في الجملتين من جانب اللاتناهي وإن أريد به عدم قصورهما عن وقوع كل جزء من أحدهما بمقابلة كل جزء من من الآخر فقد لا نسلم استحالته، فإن ذلك من عدم اللاتناهي لا من التساوي في المقدار . عن قوله : ( كالنفوس الناطقة المفارقة الفلاسفة قائلون بعدم تناهي النفوس الناطقة المفارقة الأبدان لقولهم بقدم نوع الإنسان ويدعون عدم جريان برهان التطبيق فيها إما لعدم الترتيب بينها أو لعدم اجتماعها في الوجود لأنه إن اعتبر إضافتها إلى أزمنة حدوثها يتحقق الترتيب ولا يتحقق الاجتماع في الوجود لامتناع اجتماع تلك الأزمنة وإن لم تعتبر بل أخذ ذواتها لم تكن مرتبة وأما الجواب بأنه قد يحدث منها جملة في زمان وقد يخلو زمان عن حدوث شيء منها فلا يجري التطبيق فيما بين آحادها فلا يتم لأن لنا أن نطبق بين النفوس الحادثة في أجزاء الزمان سواء كان الحادث في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء واحداً أو أكثر فإن تناهيها مستلزم لتناهي آحادها لأن الحادث في كل زمان متناه. المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة أيضاً متوقفاً على بيان كون العلة مع المعلول فيستدل به على تناهي هذه الأمور كلها.
The deficient [series] — The Fifth Observatory, Eighth Objective: The [infinite] regress is impossible, that the contingent may depend on a cause (the deficient [series] is like the excessive [series]), i.e., equal to it in the number of units, (and this is a contradiction. Otherwise), i.e., if there is not opposite every single one of the excessive one from the deficient, (then there exists in the excessive a part for which there is nothing in the deficient opposite it, and with it) i.e., with that part for which there is nothing opposite from it, (the deficient is cut off) necessarily, so the deficient becomes (finite) due to its being cut off. (And the excessive does not exceed it except by a finite amount as we depicted it, and what exceeds the finite by a finite is finite) without doubt. (Thus their being cut off and their finiteness necessarily follow in the direction in which we assumed them to be infinite and not cut off; this is a contradiction. And this proof is called the proof of alignment (burhan al-tatbiq), and it is the mainstay in refuting regress because it runs in temporal things in existence, like celestial motions, and in simultaneous things, whether there is among them a natural order like causes and effects, or a positional order like dimensions, or there is no order at all, like separate rational souls. And his saying ('i.e., equal to it, etc.') is not in the sense of absence of disparity, because there exists in each of them what exists in the other, so the part is not a part nor the whole a whole, and the existence of the excess is like non-existence; hence falls what was said: 'We do not accept the necessity of equality if by it is meant the meeting of the two series in a single limit, because the mentioned finding may be due to equality or also due to infinity; and if by it is meant absence of disparity, we do not accept its impossibility.' His saying: ('Thus the deficient is finite while it was assumed infinite; this is a contradiction.') So his saying 'and the excessive does not exceed' etc. is an additional clarification, the claim is established without it. His saying: ('and what exceeds the finite') i.e., by finite degrees. His saying: ('because it runs, etc.') So its being a mainstay is by considering the generality of regress on the side of causes. Its benefit is equal to others in proving falsehood. His saying: ('the deficient was like the excessive') i.e., equal to it because the excess is unintelligible, so it is as if it is not possible. That the cutting off of the excessive entails finiteness, and in it is the desired conclusion. But here there is a discussion: If by 'the deficient is like the excessive' is meant equality in the sense of the two series meeting at a limit, it is not necessary since there is no limit for the two series on the side of infinity; and if by it is meant their not falling short of every part of one being opposite every part of the other, we may not accept its impossibility, for that is due to non-infinity, not due to equality in magnitude. Concerning his saying: ('like separate rational souls') The philosophers claim the non-finiteness of rational souls separate from bodies due to their claim of the eternity of the human species, and they argue that the proof of alignment does not apply to them either because there is no order among them or because they do not coexist in existence because if one considers their relation to the times of their origination, order exists but coexistence in existence does not exist due to the impossibility of those times coexisting; and if one does not consider that but takes their essences, they are not ordered. As for the answer that a group of them may originate in a time and a time may be empty of any of them originating, so alignment does not apply among their individuals, it is not complete because we can align among the souls originating in parts of time whether the originated in each part is one or more, for their finiteness entails the finiteness of their individuals because the originated in each time is finite. The Fifth Observatory, Eighth Objective: The [infinite] regress is impossible, that the contingent may depend on a cause, also dependent on clarifying that the cause is with the effect, so it is used to deduce the finiteness of all these things.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains the proof of alignment (burhan al-tatbiq) as the main argument against the possibility of an infinite regress of causes or contingent beings. The author, al-Jurjani, begins by stating the claim: infinite regress is impossible for a contingent depending on a cause. The proof involves assuming two parallel series—one that is 'deficient' (ناقصة) and one that is 'excessive' (زائدة)—both assumed infinite. The excessive series has an extra part not matched in the deficient series. By aligning them unit by unit, either the deficient series completely matches the excessive (which would make the excess meaningless) or a part of the excessive has no counterpart in the deficient, causing the deficient to cut off and thus be finite. Since the excessive only exceeds the finite by a finite amount, it too must be finite. This contradicts the assumption that both were infinite, proving that actual infinity is impossible. The proof is named 'burhan al-tatbiq' and is considered the pillar (al-ʿumda) for refuting regress. It applies to both temporal sequences (e.g., celestial motions) and simultaneous things with natural order (causes and effects), positional order (dimensions), or no order (separate rational souls). The author then addresses a subtle point about the meaning of 'equality' in the proof, clarifying that the impossibility of exact equality is not about a shared limit but about the absurdity of having an unmatched part. He also discusses the case of separate rational souls: philosophers argue that these souls are infinite and that the proof does not apply due to lack of order or coexistence. Al-Jurjani counters that if one considers their origination times, order exists, and alignment can be made among souls originating in different times, leading to their finiteness. The passage concludes by reiterating the claim that the contingent depends on a cause and that the proof establishes the finiteness of all such series.

Key Terms
تسلسل
برهان التطبيق
ناقصة
زائدة
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires the two series to share a common limit.
Correction: The proof works by the impossibility of having an unmatched part in an infinite series; it does not require a limit.

Checkpoint
What is the key contradiction in Burhan al-Tatbiq?
A) The excessive series has an extra part that makes it finite.
B) The deficient series must cut off and become finite, contradicting its assumed infinity.
C) The two series cannot be aligned at all.
D) The infinite regress is impossible because causes must be finite.

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-Tatbiq proves that an actual infinite regress is impossible because aligning two parallel infinite series leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Burhan al-Tatbiq proves that an actual infinite regress is impossible, what does that mean for your own chain of decisions and actions today? Can you trace any of your habits back to an infinite chain of causes? How does that make you responsible for your choices?
?
The proof shows that adding a finite extra to an infinite series makes the whole finite. In your life, you often add 'one more' task, one more distraction, one more excuse. What if your finite moments are the only ones you have? How would you realign your priorities starting now?
?
You are finite. Every breath is a gift, not a link in an endless chain. If you truly believed that your existence is not an infinite regress, would you live differently today? What single action would you take to acknowledge that your time is limited?

Applying Burhan al-Tatbiq to Separate Rational Souls
Jurjani refutes the philosophical objection regarding infinite rational souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of alignment also applies to infinite series of separate rational souls, contrary to the philosophers' objection.

Core Idea
Even without inherent order, alignment by origination times shows finiteness.

Philosophers argued that separate rational souls are infinite and that the proof of alignment does not apply due to lack of order or coexistence. Al-Jurjani counters by considering the times of origination: souls are created successively over time, which provides a temporal order that allows alignment. This lesson examines the objection and the response, showing the proof's universal applicability.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
The passage presents the philosophers' claim that separate rational souls are infinite and the proof does not apply because there is no order or coexistence.
They base this on the eternity of the human species.
2
Identify the two reasons
The philosophers give two reasons: (1) no order among souls if considered in themselves, (2) no coexistence because they originate at different times.
3
Study Jurjani's response
Jurjani responds by considering the souls in relation to their times of origination. This introduces a temporal order, making alignment possible.
He argues that we can align souls originating in each part of time.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since time is finite in the past (according to Islamic theology), the number of souls is finite. Even if time were infinite, alignment by parts of time still leads to finiteness? Actually, the proof uses the finite nature of past time? The passage says the originated in each time is finite, so total is finite. But the argument shows the proof applies.
This demonstrates that no actual infinite can exist, even for souls.
